OIE status\n----------------------------\nThe undersigned's commentary to ProMED-mail's posting 'Influenza (64): USA A/(H3N2)v CDC report,' archive number 20120805.1228593, suggested that the subclinical or mild cases of influenza virus A/(H3N2)v recently recorded in swine in the USA are 'not regarded as OIE-notifiable swine influenza.'\n\nThis is in need of correction and clarification.\n\nSwine influenza is not a listed disease with the OIE. The current list includes the following 7 swine diseases: African swine fever, classical swine fever, Nipah virus encephalitis, porcine cysticercosis, porcine reproductive and respiratory syndrome, swine vesicular disease and transmissible gastroenteritis.\n\nAs laid out in OIE's Disease Information Summary on Swine Influenza (http://tinyurl.com/brs96wx), the reason for the disease not being listed is explained by its low mortality rate and its being a 'very uncommonly documented human disease.' Accordingly, it does not meet OIE criteria for disease notification. The criteria that are democratically adopted by all OIE Members can be found in chapter 1.2 of the OIE Terrestrial Animal Health Code; see http://www.oie.int/index.php?id=169&L=0&htmfile=chapitre_1.1.2.htm.\n\nOIE's definition of 'Emerging disease' is: 'A new infection resulting from the evolution or change of an existing pathogenic agent, a known infection spreading to a new geographic area or population, or a previously unrecognized pathogenic agent or disease diagnosed for the 1st time and which has a significant impact on animal or public health.'\n\nA non-listed animal disease is officially required to be immediately notified if determined as 'an emerging disease with significant morbidity or mortality, or zoonotic potential.' This is spelled out in Article 1.1.3.1.e of the Terrestrial Animal Health Code.\n\nIn 2009/2010, countries from 3 continents notified to the OIE diagnosed cases in swine and other animal species of 'Pandemic A/H1N1 virus (2009),' caused by the novel A(H1N1)pdm09 virus, as an 'emerging disease.' The OIE encourages members to carry out surveillance and reporting of the presence of all epidemiologic events of significance in animals due to influenza viruses.\n\nA joint OIE-FAO network of expertise on influenza, called OFFLU, was established in 2005. It initially engaged in international efforts to monitor and control infections of avian influenza in poultry and other bird species and to share biological material and data to support early development of human pandemic vaccines, and later expanded its role to cover all animal influenza viruses, including swine influenza.\n\nA chapter on swine influenza is included in OIE's Manual of Diagnostic Tests and Vaccines for Terrestrial Animals. A new version of the said chapter 2.8.8. was adopted by OIE's General Session, Paris, May 2010 (available on-line at http://www.oie.int/fileadmin/Home/eng/Health_standards/tahm/2.08.08_SWINE_INFLUENZA.pdf).\n\n--\nArnon Shimshony\nProMED-mail Animal Disease and Zoonoses Moderator\n<promed@promedmail.org>", "summary": "The undersigned's commentary to ProMED-mail's posting 'Influenza (64): USA A/(H3N2)v CDC report,' archive number 20120805.1228593, suggested that the subclinical or mild cases of influenza virus A/(H3N2)v recently recorded in swine in the USA are 'not regarded as OIE-notifiable swine influenza.' \n\nOIE's definition of 'Emerging disease' is: 'A new infection resulting from the evolution or change of an existing pathogenic agent, a known infection spreading to a new geographic area or population, or a previously unrecognized pathogenic agent or disease diagnosed for the 1st time and which has a significant impact on animal or public health.' \n\nAs laid out in OIE's Disease Information Summary on Swine Influenza (http://tinyurl.com/brs96wx), the reason for the disease not being listed is explained by its low mortality rate and its being a 'very uncommonly documented human disease.' But the states said they have not found any person-to-person transmission of the virus.\n\nIn a news release, the Indiana State Department of Health (ISDH) said the 113 cases are scattered among 18 counties and predicted that more cases would be confirmed this week. Most of the previous cases have been in children who had contact with pigs at county fairs, but today's announcement gave no details about the patients' age range, swine exposure, or severity of illness. The Ohio announcement states, 'There are currently 30 cases of H3N2v statewide; all individuals had direct contact with swine at fairs and no human-to-human passage of the virus has been confirmed.' The counties and their case numbers are Butler, 16; Clark, 3; Gallia, 4; Greene, 4; and Hamilton, 3.\n\nThe Ohio patients range from 6 months to 36 years old, the statement said. It said one patient was hospitalized as a precaution but has since been released. One other H3N2v case was reported recently in Hawaii. With the new Indiana and Ohio cases, the apparent total for the past few weeks has now reached 144. However, Tom Skinner, a spokesman for the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), cautioned that the growing case count in part reflects increased testing and that some of the cases may not ultimately be confirmed.\n\n'The increase in Indiana and soon to be other states is because states are now reporting presumptive positive cases based on testing in their labs and part of this is because of increasing numbers of samples arrived for testing compared with last week,' Skinner commented via e-mail. He added that the CDC believes most of the cases are still due to contact with pigs. 'However, limited human-to-human transmission with this virus has been observed in the past, and we expect that some human to human spread will be observed in these current outbreaks,' he said. The Indiana release said, 'Health officials have not determined person-to-person transmission at this time, but continue to investigate the possibility.'\n\nSkinner and officials from the 2 states reemphasized the need to take precautions around pigs and when attending fairs. People should wash their hands before and after touching animals and should avoid eating, drinking, or putting anything in the mouth when visiting animal areas, officials said. They also reiterated that the virus is not spread through eating properly prepared pork. The CDC has said that people who have an increased risk for flu complications, such as the elderly, small children, and those with chronic diseases, should consider avoiding pigs and swine barns this summer. 'It's important for folks to remember this is a mild illness with symptoms similar to what we see with seasonal flu,' said State Health Commissioner Gregory Larkin, MD, in the Indiana statement today. The CDC has developed a candidate vaccine for the new strain, and clinical trials of versions made by two companies are expected this fall.\n\nIndiana counties that have had H3N2v cases, according to today's announcement, are Bartholomew, Greene, Hamilton, Hendricks, Jackson, Jennings, Johnson, Kosciusko, LaPorte, Lawrence, Monroe, Morgan, Owen, Porter, Scott, Tipton, Washington, and White. A report from Indiana Public Media today said 68 people in Monroe County have contracted the virus. Penny Caudill, administrator of the Monroe County health Department, said the virus does not appear to have spread from person to person, according to the story. The swine barn at the Monroe County Fair was closed last week because of sickness among the pigs.\n\n[Byline: Robert Roos, Lisa Schnirring]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n*****\n[2]\nDate: 9 Aug 2012\nSource: Boston.com (AP) [edited]\nhttp://www.boston.com/lifestyle/health/2012/08/09/cdc-cases-new-swine-flu-strain-from-pigs/0D9SRdONnQgLtCZzFHqK5J/story.html\n\n\nCDC: 158 cases of new swine flu strain from pigs\n------------------------------------\nHealth officials Thursday reported a five-fold increase of cases of a new strain of swine flu that spreads from pigs to people.\n\nThe cumulative case count jumped from 29 a week ago to 158 this week, thanks to a wave of new cases confirmed in Indiana and Ohio, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention said. Most of the cases have been tied to state and county agricultural fairs, where visitors are put in close contact with infected pigs, said the CDC's Dr. Joseph Bresee.\n\nThe recent cases include at least 113 in Indiana, 30 in Ohio, one in Hawaii and one in Illinois, Bresee said.\n\nMost of the infected patients are children -- probably because many were working closely with raising, displaying and visiting pigs at the agricultural fairs, Bresee said.\n\nAlso, diagnosis of cases has become quicker in the last week. CDC no longer must confirm a case with its own lab. Now states are using CDC test kits to confirm cases on their own on, speeding the process along. The patients were likely infected a week or two ago.\n\nThe CDC has been tracking cases since last summer. A concern: the new strain has a gene from the 2009 pandemic strain that might let it spread more easily than pig viruses normally do.\n\nThe good news is the flu does not seem to be unusually dangerous. Almost all of the illnesses have been mild and no one has died. Two of the recent cases were hospitalized, but both recovered and were discharged, added Bresee, the agency's chief of influenza epidemiology.\n\nMore good news is that all of the recent cases appear to have spread from pigs to humans, meaning it's not very contagious, at least between people.\n\nBut there probably will be more cases in the weeks ahead, and it won't be surprising if at least a few of them involve person-to-person transmission, Bresee said.end of story marker\n\n[byline: Mike Stobbe]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Type A influenza viruses, including H3N2 and its variants, commonly infect swine, causing outbreaks among swine herds. Most of the type A influenza viruses that infect swine are genetically very different from human (seasonal) influenza viruses. While these variant swine influenza viruses seldom infect humans, such infections can and do occur. In fact, influenza viruses can spread both from swine to humans and from humans to swine. The A(H3N2)v swine influenza virus strain currently causing human infections at agricultural fairs in some parts of the United States has not so far been detected in pigs in European countries.\n\nOnly people in direct contact with infected swine, such as in swine barns and livestock exhibits housing swine at fairs, are likely to be at risk of contracting this H3N2v strain of influenza virus. However transmission of this strain of H3N2v influenza virus is thought to occur in the same way that seasonal flu transmits in people, which is mainly through coughing or sneezing by people who are infected. People also may become infected by touching something with flu viruses on it and then touching their mouth or nose. It's important to note that in most cases, variant flu viruses have not shown the ability to spread easily and sustainably from person to person.\n\nMaps:\nIndiana: http://healthmap.org/r/1DmR and http://www.digital-topo-maps.com/county-map/indiana.shtml (counties)\nOhio: http://healthmap.org/r/1BY2 and\nhttp://www.digital-topo-maps.com/county-map/ohio.shtml (counties). - Mod.CP]\n\n[We have learned that the vast majority of human H3N2v infection has been in children. This may reflect immunologic susceptibility due to lack of prior exposure to similar viruses or vaccine antigens, greater exposure of children to swine at agricultural fairs, or a combination of factors. All had attended a southern Ohio county fair.\n\nState and Butler County health departments say preliminary laboratory tests indicate possible H3N2 variant flu virus [influenza A/(H2N3)v virus], which can be contracted by humans from being around infected hogs. Officials say all 10 had contact with hogs at the Butler County Fair, which ended last weekend. The U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention is also investigating but hasn't confirmed swine flu.\n\nHealth officials say all 10 are recovering from their illness. Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap alerts\n< INFLUENZA (62): USA (OHIO), A/(H3N2)V, SUSPECTED\n*************************************************\n Indiana authorities last week said they were investigating similar cases involving 4 people who had swine contact at a northwest Indiana fair.", "reports": [{"diseases": [], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2062-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Hawaii", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Ohio", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Butler County", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Indiana", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1225864", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (61): USA (HI), A/(H3N2)v, pig worker", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1225864", "date": "2012-08-02 16:04:58", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (61): USA (HAWAII), A(H3N2)V, PIG WORKER\n**************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 1 Aug 2012\nSource: Honolulu Star Advertiser [edited]\nhttp://www.staradvertiser.com/s?action=login&f=y&id=164553586&id=164553586\n\n\nA Maui resident whose work involves contact with pigs contracted Hawaii's 1st confirmed case of an unusual swine flu strain that has been detected in only 6 mainland states in the past year, the state Health Department said Tuesday [31 Jul 2012]. The state Agriculture Department plans to take nasal swabs of a 'couple' of pig herds on Maui looking for evidence of the H3N2v virus, said state veterinarian Dr. James Foppoli.\n\nSince August 2011, the H3N2v virus has only been found in 17 patients on the mainland, according to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. 'If we find an infected herd, our recommendation is to not move the animals to make sure there's no more transmission in the herd, and then they will be OK to go to slaughter once they're well,' Foppoli said. 'Human-to-human transmission is uncommon, so that's not a likely source. You've got to suspect that there's a pig intermediate somewhere along the line where the person got the virus.'\n\nThe U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention late last week confirmed Hawaii's 1st case of the H3N2v virus after the unidentified, adult Maui resident sought medical attention upon experiencing symptoms consistent with the regular seasonal flu, including fever, cough and body aches, the Health Department said. The patient has recovered fully and did not require hospitalization, the Health Department said.\n\nHealth officials recommend that people who have been in 'close contact with pigs' consult with their health care providers if they come down with flu-like symptoms, said Health Department spokeswoman Judy Kern. She emphasized that 'pork is safe to eat if properly cooked. There is no risk at all for properly cooked pork.' State agriculture officials are encouraging Hawaii's swine producers to practice good hygiene, such as hand washing. In 2009, 13 people in Hawaii -- most of them on Oahu -- died after contracting the H1N1 'swine flu' virus that also sickened dozens of other patients.\n\nUntil the Maui case, the new H3N2v flu had been confirmed in Indiana (6 cases), Iowa (3), Maine (2), Pennsylvania (3), Utah (one) and West Virginia (2), according to the CDC. Hawaii health officials said the mainland cases occurred primarily in children and those who work closely with pigs. 'The virus seems to be behaving as previously observed in other cases, with illness similar to seasonal flu and with no sustained community transmission,' state epidemiologist Sarah Park said in a statement. 'Still, anyone who develops flu-like illness within a week after close contact with domestic pigs should see their healthcare provider.' Children, pregnant women, seniors and anyone with compromised immune systems should be particularly careful around pigs and practice good hand-washing habits, the Health Department said.\n\nBecause the Maui patient's primary care doctor participates in the Health Department's influenza like-illness sentinel network, a respiratory specimen was 1st sent to the state Laboratories Division for testing. 'Fortunately, we have a robust surveillance network, and our state laboratory detected this variant virus and conferred with federal partners,' Health Director Loretta Fuddy said in a statement. 'Thanks to the excellent cooperation of Hawaii's health care providers, participation in our sentinel network exceeds CDC recommendations,' Fuddy said. 'In addition to our state laboratory's ability to identify unusual flu strains, sentinel physicians contribute to our ability to catch incidents such as this, which might otherwise fall below the radar.'\n\n[Byline: Dan Nakaso]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nDavid Duffy\nPacific Cooperative Studies Unit\nBotany\nUniversity of Hawaii\n3190 Maile Way\nHonolulu Hawaii 96822 USA\n<dduffy@hawaii.edu>\n\n[The number of known A(H3N2)v cases in the United States has now risen to 17 in the past year. Most of the other A(H3N2)v cases in the past year have occurred in children. ' The virus seems to be behaving as previously observed in other cases, causing illness similar to seasonal influenza and with no sustained community transmission.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-30 00:00:00", "2011-08-25 00:00:00", "2009-03-25 00:00:00", "2061-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Indiana", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Iowa", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Hawaii", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Maine", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Utah", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "West Virginia", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Pennsylvania", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1228593", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (64): USA, A/(H3N2)v, CDC report", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1228593", "date": "2012-08-05 03:52:58", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (64): USA, A/(H3N2)V, CDC REPORT\n*****************************************************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 3 Aug 2012\nSource: Seasonal Influenza (Flu) ,CDC\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/flu/spotlights/h3n2v_us_cases.htm\n\n\nCDC Reports Cases 18-29 of H3N2v Virus Infection; Continues to Recommend Interim Precautions When Interacting with Pigs\n-------\nThis week CDC [US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention] reports 12 additional human infections with influenza A (H3N2) variant virus in 3 states: Hawaii (1 case), Ohio (10 cases) and Indiana (1 case). The H3N2v virus contains the M gene from the human influenza A(H1N1)pdm09 (2009 H1N1) virus, as have the previous 17 cases detected since July 2011 [last year]. All of this week's reported cases occurred in people who had direct or indirect contact with swine prior to their illness. The 10 cases in Ohio were associated with attendance at a fair where reportedly ill swine were present. The H3N2v case reported by Indiana also occurred in a person who attended a fair where swine were present. CDC continues to recommend preventive actions people can take to make their fair experience a safe and healthy one.\n\nThe number of cases of infection with H3N2v viruses with the M gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus detected in the United States since July 2011 now totals 29 [Hawaii (1), Indiana (7), Iowa (3), Ohio (10), Maine (2), Pennsylvania (3), Utah (1), and West Virginia (2)]. Twenty-three of these cases reported swine contact prior to illness onset. Among those 29 cases, 19 cases were associated with fairs where swine were present. Most human illness with H3N2v virus infection has resulted in signs and symptoms of influenza (fever, cough, runny nose, sore throat, muscle aches); 3 hospitalizations have occurred. All of the people hospitalized had high risk conditions. All H3N2v virus cases have recovered fully.\n\nAccording to USDA [US Department of Agriculture] swine influenza surveillance, this swine H3N2 virus with the pandemic [virus] M gene has been detected in swine in a number of U.S. states. This virus may be circulating widely in U.S. swine at this time. It should be noted, however, that influenza viruses have not been shown to be transmissible to people through eating properly handled and prepared pork (pig meat) or other products derived from pigs. It is possible that acquisition of the M gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus may allow H3N2v viruses to be more transmissible from pigs to people and from person-to-person.\n\nLate summer is typically fair season across the United States, and fairs are a setting that can provide many opportunities for exposures to occur between pigs and people. CDC continues to advise people to take recommended precautions when interacting with pigs or their environments, including frequent hand washing and avoiding contact with pigs that appear ill. The National Association of State Public Health Veterinarians has developed the 'Compendium of Measures to Prevent Disease Associated with Animals in Public Settings, 2011' to provide some preventive actions that are applicable to people raising swine, showing swine at fairs, or attending fairs.\n\nThis includes:\n- Wash your hands frequently with soap and running water before and after exposure to animals.\n- Never eat, drink or put things in your mouth while in animal areas and don't take food or drink into animal areas.\n- Young children, pregnant women, people 65 and older and people with weakened immune systems should be extra careful around animals.\n- If you have animals -- including swine -- watch them for signs of illness and call a veterinarian if you suspect they might be sick.\n- Avoid close contact with animals that look or act ill, when possible.\n- Avoid contact with pigs if you are experiencing flu-like symptoms.\n- If you must come in contact with pigs while you are sick, or if you must come in contact with pigs known or suspected to be infected, or their environment, you should use appropriate protective measures (for example, wear protective clothing, gloves, masks that cover your mouth and nose, and other personal protective equipment) and practice good respiratory and hand hygiene.\n\nAdditionally, in response to recent human cases of H3N2v virus infection, CDC would like to convey the following information:\n\n1. Children younger than 5 years, people 65 years and older, pregnant women, and people with certain chronic medical conditions (like asthma, diabetes, heart disease, weakened immune systems, and neurological or neurodevelopmental conditions) are at high risk from serious complications if they get influenza. These people should consider avoiding exposure to pigs and swine barns this summer, especially if sick pigs have been identified.\n\n2. Studies conducted by CDC have indicated that children younger than 10 years old would have little to no immunity against H3N2v virus, whereas adults may have some cross-protective immunity. Most cases of H3N2v have occurred in children at this time.\n\n3. There are two FDA-approved drugs that are expected to be effective in treating illness associated with H3N2v virus infection. The antiviral drugs oseltamivir (Tamiflu) and zanamivir (Relenza) - which are used to treat infection with human seasonal influenza viruses -- are also expected to be effective in treating H3N2v virus. Antiviral treatment is most effective when started as soon as possible after illness onset.\n\n4. Signs and symptoms of H3N2v virus infection cannot be differentiated from those caused by other respiratory infections, including seasonal influenza virus infection.\n\n5. Rapid influenza diagnostic tests may not detect H3N2v virus in human respiratory specimens ([they may give] false negative results). If H3N2v virus infection is suspected because of recent exposure to pigs or to an ill person who had contact with pigs, testing of respiratory specimens should be done at a state health department.\n\n6. Influenza viruses have not been shown to be transmissible to people through eating properly handled and prepared pork (pig meat) or other products derived from pigs.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Kunihiko Iizuka\n\n[While swine influenza A viruses seldom infect humans, such infections have occurred. Human infections with swine viruses are thought to occur in the same way that seasonal influenza viruses spread among people. Pigs that are infected shed influenza virus -- possibly in coughs or sneezes -- and people who are nearby can breathe the virus in. Infection also may occur by a person touching a surface or object that has virus on it and then touching their own mouth or nose.'\n\nSigns of swine flu in pigs can include fever, depression, coughing (barking), discharge from the nose or eyes, sneezing, breathing difficulties, eye redness or inflammation, and going off feed. Some pigs infected with influenza viruses, however, may have no symptoms at all.\n\nMore information about H3N2v is available on the CDC website at 'Information on H3N2 Variant Influenza A Viruses'\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/influenza-variant-viruses-h3n2v.htm.\n\nInfluenza A/(H3N2)v viruses have only been detected in North America so far. - Mod.CP]\n\n[In order to improve communications and avoid confusion, FAO, OIE and WHO established, in late 2011, a working group of experts to standardize the terminology for variant influenza viruses. The joint recommendation for the above mentioned A(H3N2) virus was: A (H3N2)v , where 'v' stands for 'variant'. Subclinical or mild cases of influenza have been reportedly observed in swine in contact with infected humans (eg a county fair in Indiana) where the A (H3N2)v strain was identified in both man and animal. These cases are not regarded as the OIE-notifiable 'Swine influenza', a highly contagious viral infection of pigs caused by pathogenic Swine Influenza Virus (SIV) strains. In some instances, SIV infections are associated with reproductive disorders such as abortion. Morbidity rates can reach 100% with SIV infections, while mortality rates are generally low. Secondary bacterial infections can exacerbate the clinical signs following infection with SIV. Transmission is through contact with SIV-containing secretions such as nasal discharges and aerosols created by coughing or sneezing.(H3N2)v causing sub-clinical or mild disease in swine, seems not to be regarded as the notifiable SIV. During 2009/2010, when the A(H1N1)pdm09 virus (Pandemic influenza A/H1N1 (2009) virus) was reportedly spreading globally in humans, becoming a cause for international concern, more than 20 countries notified the OIE about the identification of the virus in swine, as an 'emerging disease'. This virus was generally regarded to spread through the human-to-swine route. - Mod.ARN]\n\n[The Hawaiian case presents a puzzle. In ProMED post Influenza (61): USA (HI) A/(H3N2)v, pig worker, 20120802.1225864, state veterinarian Dr. James Foppoli is quoted as saying 'Human-to-human transmission is uncommon, so that's not a likely source. You've got to suspect that there's a pig intermediate somewhere along the line where the person got the virus.' Since there is no suggestion that the human had become infected during travel outside Hawaii, is that island importing pigs from the USA mainland? - Mod.JW]", "summary": "These cases are not regarded as the OIE-notifiable 'Swine influenza', a highly contagious viral infection of pigs caused by pathogenic Swine Influenza Virus (SIV) strains. All 4 individuals had direct contact with swine and all 4 cases are now recovered.\n\nThe State Health Department continues to collect information to determine the extent of the illnesses. Symptoms reported include cough, fever and sore throat. Confirmed test results on patient specimens indicate the cause as a variant influenza A virus. Swine specimens also tested positive for this virus. [Since August 2011, the US CDC has identified several human infections in 5 states with a swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) viruses -- now referred to as 'influenza A (H3N2)v, a variant influenza viruses with genes from avian, swine and human viruses.' These A(H3N2)v viruses also have the M gene from the A(H1N1)pdm09 virus. See: Update: Influenza A (H3N2)v Transmission and Guidelines -- Five States, 2011, MMWR Weekly, January 6, 2012 / 60(51);1741-1744 http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm6051a4.htm#box].\n\nInfluenza viruses can be directly transmitted from swine to people and from people to swine. Human infections are most likely to occur when people are in close proximity to live infected swine, such as in barns and livestock exhibits at fairs. Influenza viruses are not transmitted by eating pork or pork products. Since 2011, 17 human cases of [a] variant influenza A virus have been identified nationwide; 6 of these cases have been identified in Indiana.\n\nIndividuals should always wash hands with soap and water before and after petting or touching any animal. Never eat, drink, or put anything in your mouth in animal areas. Older adults, pregnant women, young children, and people with weakened immune systems should be extra careful around animals. Hoosiers who have direct, routine contact with swine, such as working in swine barns or showing swine at fairs, and have experienced cough or influenza-like illness should contact their health care provider or local health department. Symptoms include cough, sore throat, fever, body aches, and possibly other symptoms, such as nausea, vomiting, or diarrhoea.\n\nWhile influenza is not an uncommon diagnosis in pigs, the State Board of Animal Health encourages swine owners to contact a veterinarian if their animals show signs consistent with flu, including coughing, respiratory illness, off-feed and fever. Most county fairs have a private veterinary practitioner on call for on-site assistance.\n\nAdditional information regarding influenza can be found at the Indiana State Department of Health website at <www.in.gov/isdh/25462.htm>.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The report indicates that the influenza A (H3N2)v, variant virus which has genetic material originating from avian, swine and human viruses, 1st identified in Indiana and subsequently in other states, is still circulating in the state of Indiana and responsible for rare human cases of influenza disease. This virus has been associated with mild forms of influenza disease and not been detected outside the USA. - Mod.CP\n\nThe interactive HealthMap/ProMED map for Indiana is available at:\nhttp://healthmap.org/r/2qty - CopyEd.EJP]", "summary": "promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The report indicates that the influenza A (H3N2)v, variant virus which has genetic material originating from avian, swine and human viruses, 1st identified in Indiana and subsequently in other states, is still circulating in the state of Indiana and responsible for rare human cases of influenza disease. INFLUENZA (57): USA (INDIANA), A(H3N2)V\n*************************************************************\n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other"], "syndromes": ["influenza-like illness"], "event_date": ["2012-01-06 00:00:00", "2021-03-01 00:00:00", "2011-08-25 00:00:00", "2057-03-25 00:00:00", "2011-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "US", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Indiana", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1216502", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (58): USA (IN), A(H3N2)v cases, CDC", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1216502", "date": "2012-07-26 18:14:49", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (58): USA (INDIANA), A(H3N2)V, CDC\n******************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: 26 Jul 2012\nSource: CDC [edited]\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm6129a5.htm?s_cid=mm6129a5_w\n\n\nOutbreak of Influenza A (H3N2) Virus Among Persons and Swine at a County Fair\n-----------------------------------------------------------------------------\nDuring 12-16 Jul 2012, the Indiana State Department of Health and the Indiana Board of Animal Health identified respiratory illness among swine and persons at a county fair held 8-14 Jul 2012. On 16 Jul 2012, specimens were collected from 4 persons with respiratory illness; 2 had become ill on 12 Jul 2012 and sought care at an emergency department, and 2 were identified as part of the subsequent public health investigation. All 4 persons were swine exhibitors or family members of swine exhibitors and had close contact with swine.\n\nOn 18 Jul 2012, reverse transcription-polymerase chain reaction (RT-PCR) testing at the Indiana State Department of Health laboratory identified suspected influenza A (H3N2) variant (H3N2v) virus in all 4 specimens. On 21 Jul 2012, partial genome sequencing at CDC confirmed H3N2v virus with the influenza A (H1N1)pdm09 virus M gene; the viruses detected in the 4 specimens are similar to 12 viruses detected in 2011 and one detected earlier this year (1). None of the 4 persons were hospitalized, and all have fully recovered. [See also ProMED-mail archived report: Influenza (57) - USA: (IN) A(H3N2)v cases 20120725.1214922].\n\nAdditionally, all respiratory specimens collected from a sample of 12 swine at the fair were positive for influenza A (H3N2) virus. The specimens were forwarded to the National Veterinary Services Laboratories of the U.S. Department of Agriculture for additional testing. Preliminary genetic analysis has shown a very high level of similarity between the gene sequences of H3N2v viruses from humans and the H3N2 viruses from swine.\n\nAlthough human-to-human transmission of H3N2v has been limited in previous outbreaks (1), these viruses could change to transmit efficiently among humans. Clinicians who suspect influenza in persons with recent exposure to swine should obtain a nasopharyngeal swab or aspirate from the patient, place the swab or aspirate in viral transport medium, and contact their state or local health department to arrange transport and request a timely diagnosis at a state public health laboratory (1). Clinicians should consider antiviral treatment with oral oseltamivir or inhaled zanamivir in suspected cases (2).\n\nPersons who raise swine or come into close contact with swine at fairs or other venues should be aware of the potential risk for influenza transmission between swine and humans. To reduce this risk, preventive measures such as practicing frequent hand hygiene and respiratory etiquette are recommended. Persons also should avoid close contact with animals that look or act ill, when possible, and if experiencing influenza-like illness themselves, should avoid contact with swine. Additional guidelines on prevention of influenza transmission between humans and swine are available at http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/industry_guidance.htm.\n\nIncluding the cases in this report, 17 infections with H3N2v virus with the influenza A (H1N1)pdm09 virus M gene have been reported since August 2011. Novel influenza A virus infection in humans is a nationally notifiable disease (3) and a reportable disease under International Health Regulations (4). State public health laboratories should contact CDC and send all suspected novel influenza A specimens for confirmatory testing. Additional information about H3N2v is available at: http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/influenza-variant-viruses-h3n2v.htm.\n\nReferences:\n-----------\n(1) CDC. Seasonal influenza A (H3N2) viruses that circulate worldwide in the human population have significant antigenic and genetic differences from influenza A (H3N2) viruses circulating in swine. They noted that most Cambodian swine are raised on small farms in proximity to humans and other animal species.\n\nSo far, swine influenza viruses have never been isolated in Cambodia, and only rarely in surrounding countries. Antibodies against influenza A were detected in 14.9 percent of samples. The 2009 pandemic H1N1 virus was found after the pandemic virus reached Cambodia and was the most frequently found virus, peaking in 2010, followed by seasonal H1N1 and H3N2 subtypes, which peaked in 2008.\n\nResearchers found that some of the pigs had been exposed to more than one human flu virus, a factor they said could lead to reassortment events that could produce new pathogenic variants.\n\nTests on 150 random samples found no evidence of H5N1 avian influenza exposure.\n\nThe investigators concluded that more systematic surveillance systems are needed to monitor influenza A viruses on farms rather than just slaughterhouses.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nMike Nunn\n<Mike.Nunn@daff.gov.au>\n\n******\n[2]\nDate: Thu 31 May 2012\nSource: Influenza & Other Respiratory Viruses -- early view (Article first published online: 29 May 2012) [edited]\nhttp://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1750-2659.2012.00382.x/abstract\n\n\n[Ref: Rith S, Netrabukkana P, Sorn S, et al (2012): Serologic evidence of human influenza virus infections in swine populations, Cambodia. Influenza Other Respi Viruses. 2012 May 30. doi: 10.1111/j.1750-2659.2012.00382.x. [Epub ahead of print]]\n----------------------------------------------------------------------\nAbstract\n--------\nBackground:\nThis study was conducted from 2006 to 2010 and investigated the seroprevalence of influenza A viruses in Cambodian pigs, including human H1N1, H3N2, 2009 pandemic H1N1 (A(H1N1)pdm09), and highly pathogenic avian H5N1 influenza A viruses.\n\nMethods:\nA total of 1147 sera obtained from pigs in Cambodia were tested by hemagglutination inhibition (HI) assays for antibody to human influenza A viruses along with both HI and microneutralization (MN) tests to assess immunological responses to H5N1 virus. The results were compared by year, age, and province.\n\nResults:\nAntibodies against a human influenza A virus were detected in 14.9 percent of samples. A(H1N1)pdm09 virus were dominant over the study period (23.1 percent), followed by those to human H1N1 (17.3 percent) and H3N2 subtypes (9.9 percent). No pigs were serologically positive for avian H5 influenza viruses. The seroprevalence of human H1N1 and H3N2 influenza viruses peaked in 2008, while that of A(H1N1)pdm09 reached a peak in 2010. No significant differences in seroprevalence to human influenza subtypes were observed in different age groups.\n\nConclusions:\nCambodian pigs were exposed to human strains of influenza A viruses either prior to or during this study. The implications of these high prevalence rates imply human-to-swine influenza virus transmission in Cambodia. Although pigs are mostly raised in small non-commercial farms, our preliminary results provide evidence of sustained human influenza virus circulation in pig populations in Cambodia.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The current observations from Cambodia, indicative of continuous circulation of human influenza A viruses in the pig population, are in line with past observations in many countries (1). They stress the human-to-pig route, causing a subclinical infection in the animals, as widely demonstrated during the recent global spread of the 'pandemic H1N1 influenza virus' (pH1N1).\n\nReference\n---------\n1. Ottis K, Sidoli L, Bachmann PA, Webster RG, Kaplan MM (1982). Human influenza A viruses in pigs: isolation of a H3N2 strain antigenically related to A/England/42/72 and evidence for continuous circulation of human viruses in the pig population. Arch Virol. 1982; 73(2): 103-8; abstract available at http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/6184032. - Mod.AS\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/1iGB.]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n[1]\n This study was conducted from 2006 to 2010 and investigated the seroprevalence of influenza A viruses in Cambodian pigs, including human H1N1, H3N2, 2009 pandemic H1N1 (A(H1N1)pdm09), and highly pathogenic avian H5N1 influenza A viruses. \n\nConclusions:\nCambodian pigs were exposed to human strains of influenza A viruses either prior to or during this study. Our findings for 2010-11, currently in press with Clinical Infectious Diseases [1], are also relevant to Dr. Wilson's queries for the 2011-12 season.\n\nDuring the 2010-11 season, H3N2 viruses dominated among influenza detections in Canada and through phylogenetic analysis we identified two variant clades among strains circulating through the sentinel system [1]. Viruses belonging to the A/Hong Kong/2121/2010 clade comprised nearly 90% and those belonging to the A/Victoria/208/2009 clade about 10% of sequenced strains. Based on phylogenetic analysis, few belonged to the A/Perth/16/2009 vaccine clade. Both variant clades showed substantial amino acid substitutions away from the vaccine strain in key antigenic sites, meeting genetic criteria for potential antigenic drift [2]. Consistent with this, we measured suboptimal vaccine effectiveness for the H3N2 component of TIV that season [1].\n\nFor the 2011-12 northern and 2012 southern hemisphere influenza seasons, the A/Perth/16/2009 vaccine strain was retained as H3N2 component but has been replaced for the 2012-13 TIV by A/Victoria/361/2009 [3]. The latter belongs to the A/Victoria/208/2009 clade we identified through sentinel surveillance in Canada in 2010-11, but has accumulated further mutations in antigenic sites [1].\n\nIn British Columbia, we have identified more significant contribution from the A/Victoria phylogenetic clade this season and given our geographic proximity, the same may also be anticipated in the USA Pacific Northwest. The most recent surveillance bulletin for Washington state reports that 10/22 H3N2 viruses characterized to date for 2011-12 show reduced titres with antiserum produced against the A/Perth/16/2009 vaccine strain [4]. In that regard, suboptimal protection among vaccinated children this season may not be unexpected. It would be informative if the viruses detected by Dr. Wilson and colleagues were also strain characterized and sequenced to better elucidate their identity.\n\nWe should point out that influenza B has also been a contributor to influenza activity in British Columbia in recent weeks, with some lineage-level mismatch to the vaccine component noted among characterized strains, a finding also in common with Washington state [4].\n\nReferences:\n\n1. Skowronski DM, Janjua NZ, De Serres G, et al. A sentinel platform to evaluate influenza vaccine effectiveness and new variant circulation, Canada 2010-11 season. Clinical Infectious Diseases [in press].\n\n2. Bush RM, Bender CA, Subbarao K, Cox NJ, Fitch WM. Predicting the evolution of human influenza A. Science 1999;286:1921-5.\n\n3. World Health Organization. Recommended composition of influenza virus vaccines for use in the 2012-2013 northern hemisphere influenza season. Available at:\nhttp://www.who.int/influenza/vaccines/virus/recommendations/201202_recommendation.pdf\n\n4. Washington State Department of Health. Vancouver, British Columbia, Canada\n\n*****\n Both variant clades showed substantial amino acid substitutions away from the vaccine strain in key antigenic sites, meeting genetic criteria for potential antigenic drift [2].", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "influenza a/h3n2"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-24 00:00:00", "2011-12-25 00:00:00", "2012-03-25 00:00:00", "2010-11-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Vancouver", "country": "Canada"}, {"location": "Washington", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "British Columbia", "country": "Canada"}, {"location": "Canada", "country": "Canada"}, {"location": "Colorado", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "MD", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "989540", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (78): USA, swine-origin H3N2 reassortants update", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=989540", "date": "2011-12-24 14:31:52", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (78): UNITED STATES OF AMERICA, SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2\nREASSORTANTS UPDATE\n********************************************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n[1]\nDate: Fri 23 Dec 2011\nSource: Brandon Sun, The Canadian Press [edited]\nhttp://www.brandonsun.com/lifestyles/breaking-news/us-finds-new-human-infection-with-swine-h3n2-flu-virus-now-called-variant-136160753.html?thx=y\n\n\nU.S. finds new human infection with swine H3N2 flu; virus now called\n\u2018variant\u2019\n-----------------------------------------------------------------\nU.S. public health officials have found another case of human\ninfection with a swine-origin H3N2 virus, this time in a child from\nWest Virginia. And they also reported finding a human infection with a\nnew swine influenza virus never before seen in humans, in a person in\nWisconsin who had contact with pigs. The Wisconsin case seems to be a\none-off infection, with no signs of further spread. But the\ninvestigation into the West Virginia case suggests the child was\ninfected by another person, not a pig and that some amount of\nhuman-to-human spread of the virus took place in the unnamed community\nwhere the child lives, according to details released Friday by the\nU.S. Centers for Disease Control [see below - Mod.CP].\n\nThe H3N2 case is the 12th \u2013 in five states -- reported in the U.S.\nsince this new virus was first spotted in July. And the CD\nacknowledges there are probably more cases that haven\u2019t been picked\nup by the country\u2019s flu surveillance systems. 'We don\u2019t think\nthese are all' the cases, said Dr. Joe Bresee, head of the influenza\nepidemiology team in the CDC's Influenza division. To date no other\ncountry has reported seeing human infections with this virus, which\nhas also been isolated from pigs in the U.S.\n\nDetails of the investigations into the West Virginia case -- the\nsecond in that state -- and one spotted in the fall in Indiana were\nreleased late Friday [23 Dec 2011]. The Indiana case was in a man who\nworked with pigs. A separate release revealed details of the Wisconsin\ncase, which involved a new H1N1 virus with genes from avian, swine and\nhuman flu viruses. The case accounts were contained in a report that\nrevealed that international influenza science partners have agreed to\na new name for these viruses, one that drops the constant reference to\nthe viruses\u2019 swine origin. The agricultural sector objected loudly\nin the early days of the 2009 pandemic when human health officials\nreferred to the new virus as a swine influenza virus. Pork sales\nplummeted and the industry has been worried the same could happen\nagain with new reports of human infections with swine flu viruses. The\nWorld Health Organization and the UN\u2019s Food and Agriculture\nOrganization have agreed to call these viruses 'H3N2 variants' or\nH3N2v for short. The new H1N1 virus -- which has been circulating in\npigs since last year -- will be similarly called H1N1v.\n\nThe H3N2v viruses are hybrids of a swine influenza virus that has been\ncirculating for more than a decade in pigs. That virus has acquired an\ninternal gene -- the M gene -- from the H1N1 virus that caused the\n2009 pandemic. Studies have shown that gene appears to make viruses\nthat contain it more transmissible among guinea pigs, a model that is\nused to predict how flu behaves in humans. The virus from the H1N1v\ncase in Wisconsin also contains the M gene from the 2009 pandemic\nvirus.\n\nCases of human infection with the variant H3N2 virus have been seen in\nIndiana, Pennsylvania, Iowa, Maine and West Virginia. Most have been\nin children younger than 10 and none of the cases has died. Six of the\n12 had exposure to pigs but in the other six, no contact with swine\ncould be found. The CDC has said it believes some of these cases are\nthe result of human-to-human spread, but suggests spread among people\nis probably still limited at this point.\n'It\u2019s not clear yet that these viruses have acquired the ability to\ncirculate in humans in a sustained way. We certainly haven\u2019t seen\nthat in the outbreaks that we\u2019ve investigated' Bresee said. But the\nreport on the case in West Virginia suggests there may have been quite\na few more cases there. And surveillance in the area and the adjacent\ncommunities of Maryland has been ramped up.\n\nThe latest case was a child under five years of age who attended the\nsame daycare as West Virginia\u2019s earlier case of H3N2v infection. But\nthere were more than 10 days between the onset of the first case and\nthe onset of the second, which suggests the first child was not the\nsource of the second child\u2019s infection. Furthermore, multiple\ncontacts of the two children -- including other children in the\ndaycare -- were reported to have had respiratory illnesses in the\nperiod when the cases occurred, though most were not tested for flu.\n\nThis H3N2v virus is a distant cousin of the virus by the same name\nthat circulates among humans. Testing conducted at the CDC suggests\nthe virus is most closely related to human H3N2 viruses that\ncirculated in the early 1990s, which suggests many people over age 20\nmay have some antibodies that would respond to the variant virus.\n\nFlu experts are watching the situation with concern, though not alarm\nat this point. What path the virus will take remains unclear: it could\npeter out, continue to circulate at low levels, mainly in young\nchildren, or spread more broadly. Most experts don\u2019t believe it\ncould cause a pandemic, given the amount of immunity the human\npopulation has to human H3N2 viruses. But they worry it might be able\nto seed itself in humans enough to continue to spread, potentially\ncreating a situation where there might be three families of\ncirculating influenza A viruses: the H1N1s, the human H3N2s and these\nvariant H3N2s.,If it appeared the viruses were causing enough disease\nto warrant including them in the annual flu strain 'that will be a\nmess,' said Adolfo Garcia-Sastre, an influenza researcher at New\nYork\u2019s Mount Sinai Hospital. 'If they jump into humans, I don\u2019t\nthink it will be a big public health nightmare,' Garcia-Sastre said.\n'But I think what it could do is it may be able to establish a second\nlineage of H3s. And that will make it very difficult to handle, from\nthe point of view of the vaccine.'\n\n[Byline: Helen Branswell]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n\n******\n[2]\nDate: Fri 23 Dec 2011\nSource: MMWR Weekly 2011 / 60 (Early Release);1-4 [abridged & edited]\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm60e1223a1.htm?s_cid=mm60e1223a1_w\n\nUpdate: Influenza A (H3N2)v Transmission and Guidelines -- Five\nStates, 2011\n------------------------------------------------------------------\n>From 17 Aug to 23 Dec 2011, 2011, CDC received reports of 12 human\ninfections with influenza A (H3N2)v viruses that have the matrix (M)\ngene from the influenza A (H1N1)pdm09 virus (formerly called\nswine-origin influenza A [H3N2] and pandemic influenza A [H1N1] 2009\nviruses, respectively). [See the note on nomenclature change\nreproduced in the Moderator's comment below]. The 12 cases occurred in\nfive states (Indiana, Iowa, Maine, Pennsylvania, and West Virginia),\nand 11 were in children (1,2). Six of the 12 patients had no\nidentified recent exposure to swine. Three of the 12 patients were\nhospitalized, and all have recovered fully.\n\nA case in an adult male in Indiana with occupational exposure to swine\nwas among the 12, and two children in West Virginia who regularly\nattended the same day care accounted for the latest cases. This report\ndescribes those cases and swine influenza virus (SIV) surveillance\nbeing conducted by the U.S. Department of Agriculture (USDA).\n\nCase Reports\n-----------\nIndiana\n------\nOn 28 Oct 2011, CDC was notified by the Indiana Department of Health\nof a suspected case of A(H3N2)v virus infection in an adult male. The\npatient experienced onset of fever, cough, shortness of breath,\nnausea, vomiting, and body aches on October 20, and was hospitalized\nfor 4 days. He did not receive treatment with influenza antiviral\nmedications and recovered fully.\n\nOn October 22, a respiratory specimen from the patient was positive\nfor influenza at the hospital. On October 28, the virus was identified\nby real-time, reverse transcription\u2013polymerase chain reaction\n(rRT-PCR) testing at the Indiana State Public Health Laboratory as an\ninconclusive influenza A virus, consistent with results seen with\nother recent A(H3N2)v infections. On October 31, genome sequencing at\nCDC confirmed the virus as A(H3N2)v with the M gene from the\nA(H1N1)pdm09 virus, similar to the viruses identified in the other\ncases of human infection in the United States since August 2011.\n\nThe patient reported direct contact with swine during his work in the\nweek before illness onset. He said he did not wear any personal\nprotective equipment (PPE) because the swine did not exhibit signs of\nillness. No illness was reported among the patient's household members\nor other close contacts.\n\nWest Virginia\n--------\nOn November 19, a child aged <5 years developed acute onset of fever\nafter 1 week of cough and congestion. The child had been hospitalized\nfor an unrelated condition 2 days before the onset of fever. On\nNovember 21, a respiratory specimen was collected. Rapid diagnostic\ntests conducted by the hospital were negative for influenza and\nrespiratory syncytial virus, but influenza A was identified by an\nalternative rRT-PCR at the hospital. The specimen was forwarded to the\nWest Virginia Office of Laboratory Services, where it was identified\nas a suspected influenza A (H3N2)v virus. Subsequent genome sequencing\nconducted at CDC confirmed the virus as A(H3N2)v with the M gene from\nthe A(H1N1)pdm09 virus. The child, who had no recent travel or\nexposure to swine, was discharged on November 21, and has since\nrecovered from the influenza illness.\n\nAn investigation was conducted to ascertain respiratory illnesses\namong contacts of the child that occurred during November 9\u2013December\n19. Multiple contacts, including children who regularly attended day\ncare with the child, were found to have had respiratory illness during\nthis period. On November 29, a second child aged <5 years who attended\nday care regularly with the first child and who had no recent travel\nor swine exposure became ill with fever, cough, diarrhea, and\nrhinorrhea. The second child did not seek medical care and recovered\nfully from the illness. A respiratory specimen obtained from the\nsecond child on December 7 was inconclusive by rRT-PCR at the West\nVirginia Office of Laboratory Services; however, the specimen was\nconfirmed as influenza A (H3N2)v with the M gene from the A(H1N1)pdm09\nvirus via genome sequencing at CDC.\n\nNo additional A(H3N2)v cases have been identified among the other ill\nday care attendees or contacts of either patient. Enhanced\nsurveillance for influenza-like illness and increased diagnostic\ntesting of respiratory specimens is being conducted in West Virginia\nand adjacent counties in Maryland as part of the ongoing investigation\nof these cases. Currently, no evidence of additional human-to-human\ntransmission in the community has been identified.\n\nInfluenza Surveillance of U.S. Swine\n-----------------------------\nSurveillance for SIV in the United States is overseen by USDA, largely\nin swine that display influenza-like illness. In July 2009, USDA's\nAnimal and Plant Health Inspection Service and the swine industry\nimplemented a SIV surveillance program to characterize the\ndistribution of SIV in U.S. swine herds. To date, approximately 150\nSIV isolates have undergone sequencing of three genes (hemagglutinin,\nmatrix, and neuraminidase gene segments) and sequences have been\nsubmitted to GenBank. Thirty isolates have been identified as A(H3N2)\nviruses and eight of those 30 have the M gene from the influenza A\n(H1N1)pdm09 virus as determined by an informal analysis of GenBank\nsubmission data by the USDA Agricultural Research Service. Further\ncharacterization and analysis are ongoing, and new submissions are\nadded as diagnostic work is completed.\n\nMMWR Editorial Note\n-----------------\nHuman infections with the influenza viruses currently circulating\namong swine are rare. Since 2005, only 35 cases have been reported in\nthe United States, but the frequency with which they have been\ndetected increased in 2011. When different influenza viruses\nsimultaneously infect a single host (e.g., a human or swine), exchange\nof genetic material can occur, resulting in a new influenza virus.\nDepending on the antigenic distance between the new virus and recently\ncirculating seasonal viruses, little or no immunity might exist in the\nhuman population. Influenza A (H3N2)v viruses resulted from\nreassortment of influenza A (H1N1)pdm09 viruses with swine influenza A\n(H3N2) viruses. A diagram depicting this reassortment is available\nonline from CDC's Public Health Image Library [Available at:\nhttp://phil.cdc.gov/phil/details.asp (image ID: 13469]. Because these\nviruses carry a newly identified combination of genes, little\ninformation is available regarding transmission efficiency in swine,\nin humans, or between swine and humans. However, the recent human\ncases involving swine exposure and results of SIV surveillance\nindicate that these viruses also currently are circulating in swine\nherds.\n\nThe case of influenza A (H3N2)v infection after occupational contact\nwith swine in Indiana and the apparent limited human-to-human\ntransmission of A(H3N2)v virus that occurred in a day care setting in\nWest Virginia represent two different possible scenarios for\ntransmission of this virus. Work exposure highlights the risk for\ninterspecies influenza transmission in occupational settings where\nhumans are exposed to swine, an association that has been described\npreviously (3\u20137). To minimize the risk for interspecies influenza\ntransmission in occupational settings, CDC and the Occupational Safety\nand Health Administration (OSHA) encourage swine workers to 1) get\nvaccinated against human seasonal influenza, 2) wear appropriate PPE,\nand 3) practice good hygiene, such as washing hands thoroughly with\nsoap and water, when in contact with swine, especially swine that show\nsigns of illness. The National Pork Board also recommends producers\nwork with their veterinarian to develop appropriate prevention and\ncontrol measures for influenza in swine, which can include vaccinating\nswine against swine influenza. Similar to humans, swine infected with\ninfluenza viruses do not always exhibit signs of infection (8).\nPersons with swine exposure in the week before onset of an illness\nwith symptoms of influenza requiring medical care should notify their\nhealth-care provider of their swine exposures. Persons who develop\nsymptoms of influenza after close contact with swine are recommended\nto stay home until well to minimize contact with persons and swine as\nmuch as possible.\n\nThe A(H3N2)v cases in West Virginia involved two children who attended\nthe same day care, but the first child was unlikely to have\ntransmitted the virus to the second child, given the \u226510-day\ndifference in their symptom onset dates. This represents a scenario of\nlimited human-to-human transmission occurring in a day care setting.\nTherefore, clinicians also should consider the possibility of\ninfluenza A (H3N2)v infections in patients who have not had exposure\nto swine, particularly young children in those states where influenza\nA (H3N2)v cases have been reported. Clinicians who suspect variant\ninfluenza virus infection should obtain a nasopharyngeal swab, place\nthe swab in viral transport medium, and contact their state or local\nhealth department to facilitate transport and timely diagnosis (10).\nInfluenza A (H3N2)v viruses detected to date are susceptible to\noseltamivir and zanamivir for the treatment of influenza. Clinicians\nwho suspect variant influenza infection in a patient should consider\ntreatment with these medications if clinically indicated (10). Because\nthese viruses have the M gene from the influenza A (H1N1)pdm09 virus,\nthey are resistant to amantadine and rimantadine.\n\nCDC is working with USDA and state public health and animal health\nexperts in the locations where these cases have occurred to\ninvestigate each case fully and to enhance influenza surveillance to\ndetect human cases of variant influenza virus infections. The CDC\nrRT-PCR assay that was approved by the Food and Drug Administration in\nSeptember 2011 is able to identify these cases as presumptive\ninfluenza A (H3N2)v cases. These diagnostic test kits have been\ndistributed to public health laboratories in the United States and\nNational Influenza Centers designated by the World Health Organization\nin other countries. Additional rRT-PCR test enhancements to further\nimprove detection of influenza A (H3N2)v viruses are under\ndevelopment.\n\nLimited serologic studies conducted to date indicate that young\nchildren have little preexisting immunity to influenza A (H3N2)v\nviruses. Because the hemagglutinin genes of these viruses are related\nto human influenza A (H3N2) viruses that circulated in the 1990s,\nolder children and adults might have limited immunity against these\nviruses. Certain persons, including young children, pregnant women,\npersons with chronic health conditions such as asthma, diabetes, or\nheart and lung disease, and persons aged \u226565 years, are likely to be\nat greater risk for serious influenza-related complications from\nvariant influenza viruses such as influenza A (H3N2)v. The influenza A\n(H3N2)v virus is different enough from current human seasonal\ninfluenza viruses that the seasonal influenza vaccine is not expected\nto provide significant protection.\n\n[The references cited in this report can be accessed at the source\nURL. - Mod.CP]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n\n[[Future ProMED-mail reports will wherever possible conform to the new\nnomenclature described below for the swine-origin influenza A (H3N2)\nand pandemic influenza A (H1N1) 2009 viruses. - Mod.CP]\n\n'After discussions among the World Health Organization (WHO), the\nWorld Organization for Animal Health, the Food and Agriculture\nOrganization, CDC, and other U.S. federal agencies, swine-origin\ninfluenza viruses identified in humans will now be referred to as\n'variant' viruses and denoted with a 'v.' Influenza viruses identified\nin swine populations will continue to be referred to as 'swine\ninfluenza' viruses.\n\nThis change in nomenclature follows announcement by WHO of a decision\nto standardize nomenclature for the pandemic influenza A (H1N1) 2009\nvirus (which has had diverse names) as influenza A (H1N1)pdm09 [World\nHealth Organization. Standardization of terminology of the pandemic\nA(H1N1) 2009 virus. Wkly Epidemiol Rec 2011;86:480.]\n\nSince August 2011, CDC has identified 12 human infections in five\nstates with swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) viruses. Per the new\nnaming convention, these H3N2 viruses will now be referred to as\n'influenza A (H3N2) variant viruses with genes from avian, swine and\nhuman viruses,' and will be abbreviated as 'A(H3N2)v' for scientific\nuse and 'H3N2v' for general public use. These 12 A(H3N2)v viruses also\nhave the M gene from the A(H1N1)pdm09 virus.'\n\n\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at:\nhttp://healthmap.org/r/1hiS]", "summary": "CP]\n\n'After discussions among the World Health Organization (WHO), the\nWorld Organization for Animal Health, the Food and Agriculture\nOrganization, CDC, and other U.S. federal agencies, swine-origin\ninfluenza viruses identified in humans will now be referred to as\n'variant' viruses and denoted with a 'v.' Influenza viruses identified\nin swine populations will continue to be referred to as 'swine\ninfluenza' viruses. To minimize the risk for interspecies influenza\ntransmission in occupational settings, CDC and the Occupational Safety\nand Health Administration (OSHA) encourage swine workers to 1) get\nvaccinated against human seasonal influenza, 2) wear appropriate PPE,\nand 3) practice good hygiene, such as washing hands thoroughly with\nsoap and water, when in contact with swine, especially swine that show\nsigns of illness. 08:59:55", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (69): USA (IOWA) SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2 REASSORTANT\n********************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nIn this posting:\n\n[1] CIDRAP News report\n[2] Iowa Department of Health report\n\n******\n[1] CIDRAP News report\nDate: Tue 22 Nov 2011\nSource: CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease Research & Policy) News\n[edited]\nhttp://www.cidrap.umn.edu/cidrap/content/influenza/swineflu/news/nov2211swine.html\n\n\nIowa reports 3 novel swine-origin H3N2 cases\n--------------------------------------------\nHealth officials in Iowa announced today [Tue 22 Nov 2011] they have\nidentified novel swine influenza A/H3N2 infections in 3 children,\nsimilar to cases that have been identified over the past months in a\nhandful of other states [see ProMED-mail archived postings below].\n\nThe Iowa Department of Public Health (IDPH) said in a statement\n[reproduced below] that routine surveillance turned up the novel virus\nin Webster and Hamilton counties. The virus is a swine-origin\ntriple-recombinant H3N2 that includes the matrix gene from the 2009\nH1N1 virus.\n\nThough patients in other states had animal exposure or a contact who\nhad animal exposure, Iowa's recombinant H3N2 cases appear to have a\nhuman-to-human spread component, according to the statement. The IDPH\nsaid spread is so far limited, and no other cases have been identified\nin the past week.\n\nSymptoms are similar to seasonal flu and include fever, cough,\ntiredness, body aches, and loss of appetite, according to the IDPH.\nNone of the patients were seriously ill, and all have recovered. Dr\nPatricia Quinlisk, IDPH medical director, said in the statement that\nthe seasonal flu vaccine may offer some protection against the novel\nstrain, and she stressed the importance of personal protective\nmeasures such as covering coughs and sneezes and staying home when\nsick. Because the virus is unique, the IDPH said it is increasing\nsurveillance for it through clinics and emergency rooms.\n\nThe cases appear to push the nation's total to 10 triple-recombinant\nH3N2 cases reported since September [2011]. On 4 Nov 2011 the US\nCenters for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) said it confirmed the\n6th and 7th cases, in patients in Maine and Indiana. Cases have also\nbeen detected in Pennsylvania.\n\nThe CDC, however, has said that it doesn't expect the seasonal flu\nvaccine to protect against the novel strain. ECDC Risk Assessment\n------------------------------------\nConclusions and recommendations:\nFollowing recent infections in children in North America with a\nswine-origin triple reassortant influenza A(H3N2) virus that includes\na genetic component from the pandemic [H1N1] 2009 virus, and with\nprobable human-to-human transmission of these viruses, ECDC has come\nto the following preliminary opinion:\n\n- These viruses are known to be found in pigs in North America, but\nthey have not been found in pigs in Europe (EU/EEA countries).\nHowever, surveillance for influenza in pigs is weak in both North\nAmerica and Europe, and surveillance for infections in humans in close\ncontact with pigs is notably weak in Europe. Hence, all such\nstatements on the epidemiology of swine influenzas must be treated\nwith caution.\n- Most of the US cases experienced only mild disease. Those\nhospitalised had underlying conditions, and all patients recovered\ncompletely.\n- These viruses are susceptible to the neuraminidase inhibitors\n(oseltamivir and zanamivir) though the current A(H3N2) component of\nseasonal influenza vaccines is unlikely to provide protection. Older\npeople are likely to have some protection from exposure to earlier\nvaccines.\n- It is considered by the United States Centers for Disease Control\n(CDC) that there had already been some very limited human-to-human\ntransmission of these and similar viruses in the US.\n- Unlike in March 2009 (the start of the pandemic), there are no\nreports of numbers of unexplained influenza infections elsewhere in\nthe Americas. Hence, it is unlikely that these US cases represent\noutliers for a larger phenomenon.\n- Overall, therefore, the immediate direct threat to human health in\nEurope is low.\n- ECDC staff are following the situation closely and are in direct\ncontact with the WHO, the US CDC and relevant experts in EU Member\nStates.\n- There is a need to ensure that these infections could be detected\nthrough diagnostic testing in European national influenza\nlaboratories.\n- There are strong public health arguments for more active\nvirological surveillance of pig herds in Europe (and North America)\nincluding active surveillance of infections in humans that are in\ndirect or indirect contact with pigs.\n- Equally justified are more formal approaches to assessing emerging\ninfluenza viruses for their pandemic potential, and such virological\nrisk assessments should continue to be developed.\n- Unusual influenza viruses should continue to be referred to\nNational Influenza Centres and on to the WHO Collaborating Centre in\nEurope, along with relevant clinical and epidemiological data.\n\nPublic Health Issue:\n[There is] implication for Europe of recent human infection and\nlimited probable human-to-human transmission in the United States with\ntriple reassortant swine-origin influenza S-OtrA(H3N2) viruses with a\nunique gene segment combination including the M gene from the 2009\nA(H1N1) pandemic viruses.\n\nHowever, since these are now seasonal viruses and no other human\nadapted A(H1N1) viruses have been found in the last Northern and\nSouthern seasons in this risk assessment, they will generally be\nreferred to as A(H1N1) in text for brevity. Swine influenza viruses\nare generally designated as S-O to indicate swine origin.\n\nDisease background information:\nAnimal infection and disease: Swine influenza is an acute viral\ninfection of the respiratory tract in pigs. The mortality in pigs is\nlow, and recovery usually occurs within 7-10 days. An infected pig may\nhave rapid onset of fever, loss of appetite, laboured abdominal\nbreathing and coughing, but subclinical infections are common. The\ninfections are considered endemic in pigs in Europe and elsewhere in\nthe world. Serological surveys indicate that a significant proportion\nof all pigs are infected during their lifetimes. Unlike human\ninfluenza in temperate countries, there is no seasonal pattern for\ninfluenza among pigs. However, surveillance of influenza in pigs\nremains incomplete and patchy even in Europe and the USA, mostly\nbecause swine influenza has little significance for food production\nand food safety. There is little relevant legislation, and swine\nproducers are reluctant to allow voluntary testing.\n\nSwine influenza is not a notifiable animal disease in the EU, and\nthere is no extensive surveillance. The most relevant EU data come\nfrom research work in a limited number of countries supported by the\nEuropean Commission. Given the limitations of surveillance in Europe,\nstatements on the epidemiology and virology of swine influenza in\nEurope can only be preliminary. The swine influenza subtypes currently\ncirculating in pigs in Europe are known to include A(H1N1)i, A(H3N2)ii\nand A(H1N2). Recently, a reassortant pandemic A(H1N1) influenza\ninfluenza virus has been identified circulating among pigs in Germany.\nThese are swine influenzas not human influenzas.\n\nThe epidemiology and virology of swine influenza viruses in Europe and\nthe United States seem to be quite different. The triple reassortant\nviruses, S-OtrA(H3N2) seen in pigs and humans in the USA have not been\nfound in Europe to date. The main swine influenza viruses circulating\nin US pig herds in recent years have been swine triple reassortant\nA(H1N1) viruses, A(H3N2) and A(H1N2) viruses. An important point is\nthat there is little if any movement of live pigs between continents.\nIndeed, it is not legal for live pigs to be imported from the USA to\nEurope. Hence, there is little risk of spread of the American viruses\nto pigs in Europe. This is in contrast to the situation, for example,\nof the transmission of avian influenza through movement of wild birds\nand trade in domestic poultry. It is considered that there is no risk\nto humans of acquiring infections with swine influenza from\nconsumption of food.\n\nInfection with swine influenza virus has occasionally been detected in\nhumans since the 1950s. Because of weaknesses in surveillance,\nstatements on how common these infections are in humans must be made\nwith caution. Human disease is usually clinically similar to disease\ncaused by infections with human influenza viruses. Complications have\nonly rarely been reported in the literature, although they have\noccurred even in otherwise healthy adults. An exception is a well\nknown 1976 outbreak of swine influenza among young, essentially\nhealthy adult military recruits at a basic-training centre in New\nJersey (Fort Dix). This was caused by a swine-origin A(H1N1) virus and\nresulted in at least 230 infections, 13 of whom experienced severe\ndisease, and one death of a previously healthy man. After intense\nlocal transmission over a one-month period, that virus was then never\nobserved again. Hence, the pandemic 2009 A(H1N1) virus is so far the\nonly swine-origin virus that has shown the capacity to spread rapidly\nbetween humans.\n\nSerological surveys undertaken in North America among persons working\nwith pigs have shown that they quite often have evidence of prior\ninfection with swine influenza viruses. Interpretation of\nsero-prevalence data can be difficult due to cross-reactivity (i.e.\ninfection with a seasonal influenza virus might be misinterpreted as\nindicating prior swine influenza infection). There are few\ncontemporary serological data from humans for Europe [17].\n\nOverall Conclusion:\nThe viruses in question have not been seen in pigs in Europe (EU/EEA),\nbut there has been swine-to-human transmission in Europe with European\nviruses. However, there are no reports of anything but mild,\nself-limiting illness and no ongoing transmission. The clinical\npicture is in stark contrast to avian influenza A(H5N1) infections.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[By necessity, the text of this document has been severely truncated.\nInterested readers are recommended to consult the original text via\nthe source URL to view the complete text, references and citations. -\nMod.CP]", "summary": "Following recent infections in children in North America with a\nswine-origin triple reassortant influenza A(H3N2) virus that includes\na genetic component from the pandemic [H1N1] 2009 virus, and with\nprobable human-to-human transmission of these viruses, ECDC has come\nto the following preliminary opinion:\n\n- These viruses are known to be found in pigs in North America, but\nthey have not been found in pigs in Europe (EU/EEA countries). However, surveillance for influenza in pigs is weak in both North\nAmerica and Europe, and surveillance for infections in humans in close\ncontact with pigs is notably weak in Europe. \n\nDisease background information:\nAnimal infection and disease: Swine influenza is an acute viral\ninfection of the respiratory tract in pigs. The viruses in question have not been seen in pigs in Europe (EU/EEA),\nbut there has been swine-to-human transmission in Europe with European\nviruses. Unlike human\ninfluenza in temperate countries, there is no seasonal pattern for\ninfluenza among pigs.
