This was caused by a swine-origin A(H1N1) virus and\nresulted in at least 230 infections, 13 of whom experienced severe\ndisease, and one death of a previously healthy man.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2009-03-25 00:00:00", "1976-03-25 00:00:00", "1972-03-25 00:00:00", "2011-09-29 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "North \n America", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Fort Dix", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "US", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "New", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Swine", "country": "Antarctica"}, {"location": "Germany", "country": "Germany"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "968052", "headline": "PRO/AH> Influenza (74): swine-origin H3N2 reassortant, vaccine candidate", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=968052", "date": "2011-12-03 20:54:26", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (74): SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2 REASSORTANT, VACCINE CANDIDATE\n****************************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 2 Dec 2011\nSource: CIDRAP News [edited]\nhttp://www.cidrap.umn.edu/cidrap/content/influenza/general/news/dec0211vaccine-jw.html\n\n\nVaccine virus for novel H3N2 based on 2010 swine flu strain\n-----------------------------------------------------------\nThe Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) got a head start\non preparing a vaccine against the novel swine-origin influenza strain\nrecently found in 4 states by using a slightly different swine-origin\nstrain that cropped up last year, CDC officials said today. Since\nearly September, the CDC has reported 10 infections with a\nswine-origin H3N2 reassortant strain that includes the M gene from the\n2009 pandemic H1N1 (pH1N1) virus. On 22 Nov 2011 the CDC reported\nthat it had prepared a vaccine virus and was providing it to vaccine\nmanufacturers as a precaution in case the novel virus spreads.\n\nA flu update from the World Health Organization (WHO) today [2 Dec\n2011] revealed that the vaccine virus is based on an isolate that was\ncollected in Minnesota in 2010. A CDC official told CIDRAP News that\nthe isolate is from a pair of swine-origin H3N2 infections that\noccurred in Minnesota in November 2010. Those cases were among 5\nswine-origin H3N2 cases reported in 3 states during the 2010-11 flu\nseason. The 2010 strain is not an exact match for the recent novel\nH3N2 isolates, but it is close enough to be the basis for a vaccine,\nsaid Michael Shaw PhD, associate director for laboratory science in\nthe CDC's Influenza Division. 'Since we were seeing these cases\npopping up sporadically, over the summer the decision was made to go\nahead and try to make a high-yield reassortant [for a vaccine], and it\nturned out to be a good decision, to have it in reserve,' Shaw said.\nHe said it takes several weeks to prepare a flu vaccine virus. In the\ncase of the 2009 pandemic, the task took about 6 weeks, from April 15\nto the end of May, which was considered fast, he noted. The reason the\nMinnesota isolate was chosen was that it was the only one available at\nthe time that grew in eggs, Shaw explained. 'Vaccine strains need to\nbe egg-derived, because inactivated vaccines are grown in eggs,' he\nsaid. 'This one from November 2010 is not like the new reassortant in\nthat it doesn't have that M gene from the H1N1 strain. But the HA\n[hemagglutinin] is close enough to be a good vaccine match.'\n\nHemagglutinin is the viral surface protein that is recognized and\ntargeted by the human immune system. The protein often mutates, making\nit necessary to formulate new vaccines. Shaw said the hemagglutinin of\nthe 2010 isolate is very closely related to the version found on the\nrecent swine-origin viruses. 'We've characterized it genetically and\nantigenically, so it would be an acceptable vaccine strain, which is\nwhy we decided to distribute it to the manufacturers, so they can get\nsome experience with it and grow it, in case they have to scale up.'\n\nTo make a vaccine virus, the CDC first takes clinical isolates and\nputs them in eggs to see if they will grow, Shaw explained. Once an\nisolate that grows in eggs is identified, it is recombined with a\nlaboratory strain that grows well in eggs. That task is handled by a\nseparate lab at New York Medical College. The desired product is a\n'high-yield reassortant': a virus with internal genes from the lab\nstrain and surface genes from the clinical isolate. After a high-yield\nreassortant is obtained, it must be characterized to make sure it\nhasn't changed too much in the course of the lab manipulations, Shaw\nsaid. 'There's a great deal of consultation with the FDA [Food and\nDrug Administration] and the WHO to reach the conclusion that it is\nthe best one for its purposes,' he added. 'So about 6 weeks for the\npandemic one, that's a very good timeline.'\n\nSince the creation of the vaccine virus from the 2010 isolate, the CDC\nhas succeeded in growing one of the more recent novel H3N2 viruses,\nfrom an Indiana case, in eggs, but 'we don't have a high-yield\nreassortant available for it,' Shaw said. 'For now the Minnesota one\nis the only one that's egg-derived and for which we have a high-yield\nreassortant.'\n\nShaw said no more infections with the novel H3N2 strain have been\nreported since the 3 that were cited in Iowa children on Nov 22. Those\ncases were believed to involve person-to-person transmission, since\nthe children attended the same daycare center and none of them had any\nexposure to pigs. 9 of the 10 recent cases have been in children; all\nthe patients recovered. Besides the Iowa illnesses, the cases include\n2 in Indiana, 3 in Pennsylvania, and 2 in Maine.\n\n(By Robert Roos)\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n\n[It is considered by the United States Centers for Disease Control and\nPrevention (CDC)that there has already been some very limited\nhuman-to-human transmission of the swine origin H3N2 reassortant and\nsimilar viruses in the USA. Most of these cases experienced only mild\ndisease. Those hospitalised had underlying conditions, and all\npatients recovered completely.\n\nAccording to the European Centre for Disease Prevention and Control\n(ECDC), although such viruses are known to be present in pigs in North\nAmerica, none have been found in pigs in Europe (EU/EEA countries).\nHowever, surveillance for influenza in pigs is weak in both North\nAmerica and Europe. (See ProMED-mail archived report below on risk\nassessment).\n\nNonetheless the preemptive development of a candidate vaccine virus by\nthe US CDC is a prudent and commendable initiative. - Mod.CP]\n\n]", "summary": "INFLUENZA (74): SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2 REASSORTANT, VACCINE CANDIDATE\n****************************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n \nThe Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) got a head start\non preparing a vaccine against the novel swine-origin influenza strain\nrecently found in 4 states by using a slightly different swine-origin\nstrain that cropped up last year, CDC officials said today. \n\nSince the creation of the vaccine virus from the 2010 isolate, the CDC\nhas succeeded in growing one of the more recent novel H3N2 viruses,\nfrom an Indiana case, in eggs, but 'we don't have a high-yield\nreassortant available for it,' Shaw said. ' The 2010 strain is not an exact match for the recent novel\nH3N2 isolates, but it is close enough to be the basis for a vaccine,\nsaid Michael Shaw PhD, associate director for laboratory science in\n Since\nearly September, the CDC has reported 10 infections with a\nswine-origin H3N2 reassortant strain that includes the M gene from the\n2009 pandemic H1N1 (pH1N1) virus. Since we were seeing these cases\npopping up sporadically, over the summer the decision was made to go\nahead and try to make a high-yield reassortant [for a vaccine], and it\nturned out to be a good decision, to have it in reserve,' Shaw said. [It is considered by the United States Centers for Disease Control and\nPrevention (CDC)that there has already been some very limited\nhuman-to-human transmission of the swine origin H3N2 reassortant and\nsimilar viruses in the USA. We've characterized it genetically and\nantigenically, so it would be an acceptable vaccine strain, which is\nwhy we decided to distribute it to the manufacturers, so they can get\nsome experience with it and grow it, in case they have to scale up.' After a high-yield\nreassortant is obtained, it must be characterized to make sure it\nhasn't changed too much in the course of the lab manipulations, Shaw\nsaid. ' Those\ncases were believed to involve person-to-person transmission, since\nthe children attended the same daycare center and none of them had any\nexposure to pigs. On 22 Nov 2011 the CDC reported\nthat it had prepared a vaccine virus and was providing it to vaccine\nmanufacturers as a precaution in case the novel virus spreads. The desired product is a\n'high-yield reassortant': a virus with internal genes from the lab\nstrain and surface genes from the clinical isolate. Shaw said the hemagglutinin of\nthe 2010 isolate is very closely related to the version found on the\nrecent swine-origin viruses. ' Those cases were among 5\nswine-origin H3N2 cases reported in 3 states during the 2010-11 flu\nseason. [2 Dec\n2011] revealed that the vaccine virus is based on an isolate that was\ncollected in Minnesota in 2010. A CDC official told CIDRAP News that\nthe isolate is from a pair of swine-origin H3N2 infections that\noccurred in Minnesota in November 2010. Vaccine strains need to\nbe egg-derived, because inactivated vaccines are grown in eggs,' he\nsaid. '", "reports": [{"diseases": [], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2009-03-25 00:00:00", "2021-04-15 00:00:00", "2010-03-25 00:00:00", "1974-03-25 00:00:00", "2011-03-25 00:00:00", "2010-11-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "North", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Minnesota", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Iowa", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "US", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Maine", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Indiana", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Pennsylvania", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "North \n America", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "957787", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (70): USA (IA) swine-origin H3N2 reassortant", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=957787", "date": "2011-11-24 10:55:52", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (70): USA (IOWA) SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2 REASSORTANT\n********************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 23 Nov 2011\nSource: CDC. MMWR Morb Mortal Wkly Rep 2011; 60 (dispatch); 1-3\n[edited]\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm60d1123a1.htm?s_cid=mm60d1123a1_e&source=govdelivery\n\n\nLimited human-to-human transmission of novel influenza A (H3N2) virus\n- Iowa\n----------------------------------------------------------------------\nOn 20 Nov 2011 CDC confirmed 3 cases of swine-origin triple\nreassortant influenza A (H3N2) (S-OtrH3N2) virus infection in children\nin 2 counties in Iowa. None of the children were hospitalized, and\neach has recovered from a mild episode of febrile respiratory illness.\nAll 2 were in contact with one another, and none had a known recent\nexposure to swine. No additional human infections with this virus have\nbeen detected in Iowa, and no evidence of sustained human-to-human\ntransmission of this S-OtrH3N2 virus exists; surveillance is ongoing.\n\nIn total, 18 human infections with swine-origin influenza A (H3N2)\nviruses have been identified since 2009 (1,2). The most recent 10\ncases, including the 3 Iowa cases described in this report, were\ninfections with S-OtrH3N2 viruses containing the matrix (M) gene from\nthe pandemic 2009 influenza A (H1N1) virus (pH1N1). These viruses are\nconsidered reassortant viruses between a swine-origin influenza A\n(H3N2) virus circulating in North American swine and a pH1N1 virus.\nAll cases of human infection with S-OtrH3N2 virus containing the M\ngene from the pH1N1 virus have occurred in 2011 and have been reported\nfrom 4 states: Pennsylvania (3 cases), Maine (2), Indiana (2), and\nIowa (3) (3).\n\nCase reports\n------------\nPatient A: in the 2nd week of November 2011, patient A, a previously\nhealthy female child, experienced acute onset of influenza-like\nillness (ILI). 3 days after her illness onset (illness day 4), she was\nseen by a health-care provider, who obtained a respiratory specimen\nand performed a rapid influenza diagnostic test, which was positive.\nAs part of routine influenza surveillance, the respiratory specimen\nwas forwarded to the University of Iowa State Hygienic Laboratory\n(SHL) for further evaluation. Patient A's brother experienced onset of\nILI one day before patient A's date of illness onset. Patient A's\nbrother was not tested for influenza but was treated with oseltamivir\nby a health-care provider and has recovered. During her illness days 2\nand 3, patient A was in contact with her father, who subsequently\ndeveloped ILI 2 days after his most recent contact with patient A. He\nwas not tested for influenza. No other household member has reported\nrespiratory illness. No family member reported exposure to swine\nbefore their illness onset. On her illness day one, patient A attended\na small gathering of children.\n\nPatients B and C: patient B is a previously healthy male child who\ndeveloped ILI 2 days after patient A's 1st day of illness. He is the\nsibling of patient C, a previously healthy male child who developed\nILI one day after patient B's illness onset. Both children were seen\nby a health-care provider 2 days after patient B's illness onset;\nrapid influenza diagnostic testing was positive for both patients. As\npart of routine influenza surveillance, respiratory specimens were\nforwarded to SHL for further evaluation. The mother of patients B and\nC reported that no other household member had a respiratory illness\nand none had been exposed to swine before patient B became ill. On\npatient A's illness day one, patients B and C attended the same small\ngathering of children as patient A.\n\nEpidemiologic and laboratory investigations\n-------------------------------------------\nAn investigation by the Iowa Department of Public Health (IDPH)\ndetermined that the families of patients A, B, and C reported no\nrecent travel or attendance at community events. To date, the only\nepidemiologic link among patients A, B, and C that has been identified\nis attendance at a gathering of children on patient A's illness day\none. No illnesses were reported among adults or among the 5 other\nchildren who were present at this gathering on that day. No swine\nexposures have been identified among adults or children attending this\ngathering. IDPH has detected no increase in absenteeism or reports of\nrespiratory illness in the community where patients A, B, and C reside\nor in the schools in the community. Enhanced surveillance for ILI has\nbeen implemented in health-care facilities in the communities where\npatients A, B, and C reside. IDPH has instructed health-care providers\nto obtain respiratory specimens from patients with ILI for influenza\ndiagnostic testing at SHL. Thus far, no additional cases of S-OtrH3N2\ninfection have been identified, and surveillance data from the state\nhave shown low levels of influenza activity currently and at the time\nof all these patients' illnesses.\n\nEight days after patient A's illness onset, real-time reverse\ntranscription-polymerase chain reaction (rRT-PCR) testing of\nrespiratory specimens from patients A, B, and C at SHL indicated\npossible S-OtrH3N2 influenza virus. At CDC, preliminary rRT-PCR\ndiagnostic results were inconclusive but indicated probable infection\nwith a swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) virus. Subsequent complete\ngenome sequencing at CDC confirmed all 3 specimens as S-OtrH3N2 with\nthe M gene from the pH1N1 virus. The viruses from these 3 patients are\nresistant to amantadine and rimantadine but are expected to be\nsusceptible to the neuraminidase inhibitor drugs oseltamivir and\nzanamivir based on their genetic sequence. Because these viruses carry\na unique combination of genes, little information currently is\navailable regarding the capacity of this virus to transmit efficiently\nin swine, humans, or between swine and humans.\n\nMMWR editorial note\n-------------------\nSince July 2011, a total of 10 cases of human infection with S-OtrH3N2\nviruses have been identified in the United States, all containing the\nM gene from the pH1N1 virus. Seven of these 10 cases resulted in mild\nillness, but 3 of the infected persons were hospitalized for\ninfluenza; all patients have recovered. In all 7 earlier cases,\nexposure to swine was identified in the patient or in a close contact\nof the patient (4). The lack of known exposure to swine in the 3 cases\ndescribed in this report, combined with the known epidemiologic links,\nsuggests that limited human-to-human transmission of this novel\ninfluenza virus might have occurred. Transmission of swine-origin\ninfluenza A (H3N2) viruses not containing the M gene from the pH1N1\nvirus to humans from close contact with an infected person has been\nreported previously and has not resulted in sustained human-to-human\ntransmission (5). Preliminary evidence from the investigation of these\ncases in Iowa shows no evidence of ongoing transmission among humans.\nSwine influenza viruses are spread from pig to pig but are not known\nto spread through human contact with pork or pork products.\n\nAlthough the vast majority of human infections with animal influenza\nviruses do not result in human-to-human transmission (6), each case\nshould be investigated fully to ascertain if these viruses are\ntransmitted among humans and to limit further exposure of humans to\ninfected animals, if infected animals are suspected. Such\ninvestigations require close collaboration among state, local, and\nfederal public and animal health officials. As part of routine\npreparedness measures to counter possible pandemic threats posed by\nnovel influenza viruses in the event that they gain the ability to\nspread easily from person-to-person, CDC has developed a candidate\nvaccine virus that could be used to produce a human influenza vaccine\nagainst these S-OtrH3N2 viruses and has provided this candidate virus\nto manufacturers.\n\nAlthough swine exposure was not associated with the 3 cases described\nin this report, because most previous cases of human infection with\nS-OtrH3N2 viruses have occurred in patients who reported swine\nexposure before illness onset, clinicians should consider swine-origin\ninfluenza A virus infection in the differential diagnosis of patients\nwith febrile respiratory illness who have had contact with swine. It\nis anticipated that commercially available diagnostic tests, including\npoint-of-care rapid tests, will detect infection with the S-OtrH3N2\nvirus; however, these tests will not differentiate S-OtrH3N2 from\nseasonal influenza A viruses. Clinicians who suspect swine influenza\nvirus infections in humans should treat with oseltamivir when\nindicated (7), obtain a nasopharyngeal swab from the patient, place\nthe swab in viral transport medium, and contact their state or local\nhealth department to facilitate transport and timely diagnosis at a\nstate public health laboratory, using the CDC RT-PCR assay cleared by\nthe Food and Drug Administration. CDC requests that state public\nhealth laboratories send all suspected novel influenza A specimens,\nsuch as these S-OtrH3N2 viruses, to the CDC Influenza Division's Virus\nSurveillance and Diagnostics Branch Laboratory.\n\nThe 2011-12 seasonal influenza vaccine is expected to provide limited\nprotection from this virus for adults but none for young children.\nEnhanced surveillance, including surveillance for ILI and diagnostic\ntesting of respiratory specimens, is being conducted in Iowa and\nsurrounding states as part of the ongoing investigation of these\ncases. Additional information about swine influenza is available at\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu.\n\nReferences\n----------\n1. CDC. Update: influenza activity - United States, 2010-11 season,\nand composition of the 2011-12 influenza vaccine. MMWR 2011; 60:\n705-12. Available at\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm6021a5.htm?s_cid=mm6021a5_w.\n2. CDC. Update: influenza activity - United States, 2009-10 season.\nMMWR 2010; 59: 901-8. Available at\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm5929a2.htm?s_cid=mm5929a2_w.\n3. CDC. FluView: 2011-2012 influenza season week 45 ending 12 Nov\n2011. Consultation rates for influenza-like illness (ILI) and acute respiratory infection (ARI) continue to be relatively low in most countries of the Region, but with many countries reporting increasing activity.\n\u2022 29 % of sentinel specimens tested positive for influenza, which is an increase over last week: 95% of these were influenza A.\n\u2022 Of the 352 influenza A viruses from sentinel sources that were subtyped, 99 % were A(H3N2).\n\nCurrent situation: week 3/2012\n\nTrends in clinical activity were reported by 42 countries: 20 stable, 19 increasing and 3 decreasing. At the same time, ILI and ARI consultation rates remained at low levels in most countries of the WHO European Region. Similar to the previous week, consultation rates were highest in young children. Of the 41 countries reporting on the geographical spread of influenza: 12 reported no activity; 7 local activity; 18 sporadic activity; and 4 countries (Finland, Iceland, Italy and Spain) regional activity. Intensity was reported to be low in most countries reporting on this indicator, except for Bulgaria, Iceland, Italy and Spain, where influenza activity was at medium levels.\n\nSevere acute respiratory infection (SARI): week 3/2012\n\n11 countries reported data from sentinel hospital-based surveillance for SARI: Albania, Armenia, Belarus, Georgia, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, the Republic of Moldova, Romania, the Russian Federation, Serbia and Ukraine. The number of SARI hospitalizations remained largely unchanged compared to the previous week, except in Kazakhstan, which reported increases in SARI hospitalization \u2013 mainly in children aged 0\u20134 years. A total of 122 respiratory specimens was collected from SARI patients, of which 10 (8%) tested positive for influenza with 6 being subtyped, all as A(H3N2). 6 countries so far (Albania, Belarus, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, Romania and Ukraine) have reported influenza detections in SARI patients during the course of this influenza season.\n\nVirological situation: week 3/2012\n\nSentinel outpatient clinics collected 1469 respiratory specimens, 422 (29 %) of which tested positive for influenza viruses: 401 (95%) were type A and 21 (5%) were type B. Of the 352 influenza A viruses subtyped, 349 (99%) were influenza A(H3N2) and 3 (1%) were A(H1N1)pdm09. In the 18 countries testing 20 or more sentinel specimens, influenza positivity ranged from 0% to 85%, with a median of 22% (mean: 25%). In addition, 525 non-sentinel specimens were reported positive for influenza: 504 (96%) type A and 21 (4%) type B. Of the influenza A viruses, 189 were subtyped: 173 (92%) as A(H3N2) and 16 (8%) as A(H1N1)pdm09.\n\nCumulative virological data: weeks 40/2011 \u2013 3/2012\n\nDuring this period, 3436 influenza virus detections were reported: 3226 (94%) were influenza A and 210 (6%) were influenza B. find\n-------------------------------------------------\nA new swine flu virus with human genes has again been found in\nmainland pigs at a Hong Kong slaughterhouse. But the swine flu H3N2\nvirus is unlikely to cause a 'major' human health risk, the Centre\nfor Food Safety said. 15 pigs were found infected with 'essentially a\nswine influenza H3N2 virus that has picked up some genes of human\nswine influenza virus,' the center said. They were detected out of\n1000 samples taken from August to mid-October [2011].\n\nThe same flu subtype [had been] isolated also in the last round of the\nsurveillance programs, from May to July 2011, in 16 pigs. In both\ninstances, the department said it had notified mainland authorities.\nThere have been no recent reports of such H3N2-infected swine in\nGuangdong province, however.\n\nFlu expert Malik Peiris said the H3N2 subtype seen in the pigs is not\nthe same swine-originated new H3N2 that has infected 7 people in the\nUnited States since July [2011]. The new cases, in Maine and Indiana,\nbring to 7 the number seen in the U.S. since July [2011]. To date\nCanada has seen no infections with this virus, the National\nMicrobiology Laboratory said.\n\nWhile the infections have been mainly mild, the CDC is keeping a close\neye on the situation. The head of the Atlanta-based agency's influenza\ndivision said a seed strain for a vaccine that would protect against\nthis virus has already been developed and has been given to vaccine\nmanufacturers. 'We're really trying to be in front of events in terms\nof preparedness,' Dr. Nancy Cox said in an interview. 'We're being\nvery vigilant because we realize that there's a portion of the\npopulation ... that really has very little cross-reactive antibodies\nto the swine reassortant virus.'\n\nThe group Cox was referring to is children. Most of the infections --\n2 from Maine, 3 from Pennsylvania and 2 from Indiana - have been in\nyoung children, though Cox noted one of the cases was a 59-year-old\nperson. Most of the people who have been infected had close contact\nwith pigs and the 2 latest cases are no exception.\n\nDr. Stephen Sears, state epidemiologist for Maine, said the 2 children\nfrom his state who were infected both had a lot of contact with pigs.\nWhile they live in the same region of Maine, the children did not have\ncontact with each other. Sears said there is no thought at this point\nthat the virus is spreading from person to person and there were no\nreports of influenza among members of either child's family.\n\nThe virus is a strain of influenza A known as H3N2 that has picked up\none of the genes -- the M gene -- of the H1N1 flu strain that caused\nthe 2009 pandemic. The CDC says the swine H3N2 virus probably picked\nup the M gene from the pandemic H1N1 virus when a pig was co-infected\nwith swine H3N2 and the H1N1 strain. It's not clear whether having\nthat gene is helping this virus transmit to humans. But a study done\nin guinea pigs -- one of the animal models for influenza -- showed\nthat the M gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus is critical to the pandemic\nvirus's ability to transmit well [in guinea pigs].\n\nDr. Adolfo Garcia-Sastre, who was involved in that study, said that\nfinding is suggestive, but guinea pigs aren't people. 'Whether these\ncases now reflect that this particular H3N2 with the M gene of the new\nH1N1 transmits better into humans, I think it's too early to say,'\nsaid Garcia-Sastre, a researcher at New York's Mount Sinai Hospital.\n\nThe swine-origin H3N2 is related -- though not closely - to H3N2\nviruses that circulate each winter among people. The CDC said the H3N2\ncomponent in the annual flu shot would not be expected to protect\nagainst the swine-origin variety. Cox said the virus seems most\nclosely related to human H3N2s that circulated in the early 1990s.\nThat suggests a lot of people would probably have antibodies against\nthis virus, which in turn might make it hard for the virus to be able\nto spread person to person. But people born after the early 1990s\nprobably would not be protected against the virus. Cox said it's not\npossible to be sure, but based on lab work, her team believes fewer\npeople would be susceptible to this virus than were vulnerable to the\n2009 pandemic virus when it emerged.\n\nPigs are highly susceptible to influenza, and a number of flu strains\ncirculate among swine herds. Occasionally, these viruses trigger human\ninfections, generally among people who work with pigs. There have been\n28 such cases reported since 2005, the CDC said.\n\nIn the last few years, the rate of such detections appears to have\nincreased. But no one is sure whether that means there have been more\ncases, or whether there have always been these numbers of cases but\nimprovements to surveillance means they are now being seen.\n\nThe United States and other countries have built up flu surveillance\nefforts in the past 7 or 8 years, 1st due to concern over H5N1 avian\nflu and later as a result of the 2009 pandemic. After a spate of human\ncases of swine H3N2 infection last year [2010], the CDC asked the\nlaboratory that makes candidate viruses for vaccines to make one for a\nswine H3N2 vaccine. That lab is at New York Medical College in\nValhalla. That virus had the same hemagglutinin and neuraminidase\ngenes -- the H and N in a virus's name -- as the new virus does. And\nflu vaccines target [the proteins encoded] in these 2 genes.\n\n[Byline: Helen Branswell]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[There are now 7 cases (6 children and an adult) of infection with an\ninfluenza virus that has been sporadically jumping to people from pigs\nin the United States; 2 in Maine, 3 in Pennsylvania and 2 in Indiana.\nAlthough these cases appear to be independent events, most of those\ninfected had close contact with pigs.\n\nAt present there is no evidence that the virus is spreading from\nperson to person and there has been no report of influenza among\nfamily members.\n\nIt seems likely that the swine H3N2 virus probably picked up its M\ngene (by sub-unit reassortment) from the pandemic H1N1 virus when a\npig was co-infected with swine H3N2 and the H1N1 strain. Model\nexperiments with guinea pigs suggest that the presence of the M gene\nfrom the 2009 H1N pandemic virus favours transmissibility of the the\nH3N2 reassortant virus.\n\nThe infections have been mainly mild, but as a precaution the\ninfluenza division of the CDC has already developed a seed strain for\na vaccine that would protect against this virus and supplied it to\nvaccine producers.\n\nA map of the states of the United States of America can be accessed\nat:\nhttp://www.50states.com/cap.htm. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "REASSORTANT, UPDATE\n*******************************************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n But a study done\nin guinea pigs -- one of the animal models for influenza -- showed\nthat the M gene from the 2009 H1N1 virus is critical to the pandemic\nvirus's ability to transmit well [in guinea pigs]. The child had contact with\npigs, including time spent in a closed setting at an agricultural\nfair, public health officials said. The child became sick earlier this\nmonth [October 2011]. So far, officials said Friday [21 Oct 2011], the\ncase of the child in Cumberland County, Maine, 'is an isolated event.\nThey said Maine 'is not aware of any person-to-person transmission.'\n\nPublic health officials have long feared a new, dangerous strain of\ninfluenza would be bred in animals and passed to people, where it\nwould have the ability to spread throughout the human population in a\nmanner similar to the devastating 1918 influenza pandemic. That\nconcern explains the enormous publicity and reaction to the so-called\nswine flu scare of 2009, which petered out when the H1N1 strain proved\nless dangerous than feared.\n\nThe Maine Center for Disease Control and Prevention and federal health\nauthorities are investigating the latest case, which is unusual,\nalthough not unique. Since 2005, the federal Centers for Disease\nControl and Prevention reports, there have been 26 cases of flu virus\npassing from pigs to people. None of those cases have resulted in\ndisease that passes from people to other people.\n\n'Swine-origin influenza is rare in humans, but veterinarians should\nremind owners and those who work closely with swine to watch for\nsymptoms of ILI (influenza-like illness) in themselves and their\nfamilies,' said the announcement from Maine, which was passed on to\nNew Hampshire veterinarians on Thursday [20 Oct 2011]. 'Any person\nwith ILI, defined as fever greater than 100 deg F [38 deg C] with\ncough or sore throat, should contact their primary care provider for\nfollow-up.'\n\nInfluenza can have a variety of signatures, differentiated by the\nletters H and N, which stand for different proteins on the surface of\nthe virus. There are 16 types of H (hemagglutinin) and 9 types of N\n(neuraminidase), meaning up to 1454 strains of flu can exist, which is\nwhy tracking and vaccinating for the disease is difficult.\n\nOfficials said the influenza in the Maine case is similar to rare\nhuman infections with swine-origin H3N2 viruses, but it also contains\na genetic component of the pandemic H1N1 virus. This is genetically\nsimilar to 4 previous cases identified in the United States this year\n[2011], 3 in Pennsylvania and one in Indiana, all of which had\nexposure to swine.\n\n[Byline: David Brooks]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This press report has been incorrectly interpreted elsewhere as\nconfirmation of transmission of a swine-origin reassortant A/H3N2\ninfluenza virus to humans from pigs in New Hampshire. Transmission of\nthis strain of influenza virus occurred in isolated cases in the\nstates of Indiana, Maine, and Pennsylvania, with no onward\nhuman-to-human transmission. These cases are described in detail in\nthe ProMED-mail references below.\n\nNo transmission (or isolation) of this swine-origin reassortant H3N2\ninfluenza virus has occurred in the state of New Hampshire as far as\nis known at present.\n\nThe HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of the state of Maine can be\naccessed at http://healthmap.org/r/1m1U. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "REASSORTANT\n****************************************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n \n\n'Swine-origin influenza is rare in humans, but veterinarians should\nremind owners and those who work closely with swine to watch for\nsymptoms of ILI (influenza-like illness) in themselves and their\nfamilies,' said the announcement from Maine, which was passed on to\nNew Hampshire veterinarians on Thursday \n\nPublic health officials have long feared a new, dangerous strain of\ninfluenza would be bred in animals and passed to people, where it\nwould have the ability to spread throughout the human population in a\nmanner similar to the devastating 1918 influenza pandemic. That\nconcern explains the enormous publicity and reaction to the so-called\nswine flu scare of 2009, which petered out when the H1N1 strain proved\nless dangerous than feared. Any person\nwith ILI, defined as fever greater than 100 deg F [38 deg C] with\ncough or sore throat, should contact their primary care provider for\nfollow-up.' promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This press report has been incorrectly interpreted elsewhere as\nconfirmation of transmission of a swine-origin reassortant A/H3N2\ninfluenza virus to humans from pigs in New Hampshire. Officials said the influenza in the Maine case is similar to rare\nhuman infections with swine-origin H3N2 viruses, but it also contains\na genetic component of the pandemic H1N1 virus. this strain of influenza virus occurred in isolated cases in the\nstates of Indiana, Maine, and Pennsylvania, with no onward\nhuman-to-human transmission. No transmission (or isolation) of this swine-origin reassortant H3N2\ninfluenza virus has occurred in the state of New Hampshire as far as\nis known at present. \n\nInfluenza can have a variety of signatures, differentiated by the\nletters H and N, which stand for different proteins on the surface of\nthe virus. Since 2005, the federal Centers for Disease\nControl and Prevention reports, there have been 26 cases of flu virus\npassing from pigs to people.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other"], "syndromes": ["influenza-like illness"], "event_date": ["2021-03-26 00:00:00", "2021-03-25 00:00:00", "2005-03-25 00:00:00", "2011-10-25 00:00:00", "1918-03-25 00:00:00", "2009-03-25 00:00:00", "2063-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "New Hampshire", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "MAINE", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Cumberland County", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Maine", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Pennsylvania", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Indiana", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "748888", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Influenza (40): H3N2/H1N1 reassortant ex patient", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=748888", "date": "2011-06-09 12:24:06", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (40): H3N2/H1N1 REASSORTANT ex PATIENT\n************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 8 Jun 2011\nFrom: Jonathan Gubbay <Jonathan.Gubbay@oahpp.ca> [edited]\n\n\nReassortment following coinfection with seasonal H3N2 and pandemic\n(H1N1) 2009 viruses in Ontario, Canada\n----------------------------------------------------------------------\nA 16-month-old infant was admitted to a Greater Toronto Area Hospital\non 24 Jan 2011 with respiratory and gastrointestinal symptoms. After\n15 hours, he was discharged home and subsequently recovered\nuneventfully. 2 nasopharyngeal swabs were collected on the day of\nadmission and sent to the Ontario Agency for Health Protection (OAHPP)\nPublic Health Laboratories (PHL) for influenza testing by real-time\nreverse transcriptase PCR (rRT-PCR). Influenza A [virus] was detected\nin both specimens by rRT-PCR, and when subtyped were positive for\nhemagglutinin (H3) gene in a moderately high copy number (cycle\nthresholds 29 and 31). They were also noted to be positive for the\npandemic (H1N1) 2009 (pH1N1) neuraminidase (NA) gene at a very low\nlevel (CT values 38 and 39), which was detected using an in-house\nrRT-PCR (1). Suspecting contamination, primary samples were\nreextracted and retested; identical results were obtained.\n\nFurther sequencing confirmed presence of the following genes in the\nprimary samples:\nA. Pandemic H1N1 2009 genes: Matrix, NS, H1, N1\n(H1 sequencing was conducted on one primary sample only)\nB. Seasonal H3N2 genes: N2\n(H3 sequencing was not conducted on either primary sample)\n\nThe 1st specimen underwent conventional viral culture in rhesus\nmonkey kidney cells. Whole genome sequence analysis of cultured\nmaterial showed that the isolate possessed H3 and N2 of seasonal H3N2\nin combination with PB2, PB1, PA, NS, NP, and M of pandemic H1N1. Gene\nsequences obtained in culture and primary specimen were identical. A\nBLAST [basic local alignment search] was conducted against sequences\navailable in GenBank. The H3 and N2 gene sequences most closely\nmatched the currently circulating A/Perth/16/2009 strain.\n\nThe matrix and NS genes amplified from the primary sample were\nidentical to currently circulating pH1N1, most closely matching the\nA/California/07/2009 strain.\n\nCanada's National Microbiology Laboratory performed plaque forming\nassays on the primary sample and culture material using Madin-Darby\ncanine kidney cell lines. Sequencing of individual plaque material in\nboth primary sample reconfirmed a reassortant virus as described\nabove, with no evidence of the HA and NA genes of pH1N1. In addition,\ngene sequences obtained in both plaque assays matched each other, and\nwere also identical to those obtained at OAHPP. The isolate strain\ntype was A/Perth/16/09 (H3N2) by hemagglutination inhibition assay.\n\nSummary and implications\n------------------------\nThis case represents coinfection with H3N2 and pH1N1, followed by\nreassortment in the patient (in vivo reassortment). The low level of\npandemic H1N1 2009 NA gene in the primary sample indicates that the\nreassortment occurred in the child and the lab detected a remnant of\nNA left behind. If it were just coinfection but not reassortment, we\nwould have expected to find evidence of H3N2 viral genes other than H3\nand N2 in the primary sample. The subsequent reassortant virus\nconsisted of HA and NA of H3N2 together with the remaining 6 genes\n(PB2, PB1, PA, NS, NP and M) of pH1N1.\n\nTo the best of our knowledge, this is the 1st case of reassortment\ninvolving pH1N1 and seasonal H3N2. We could not document any\ntransmission of this reassortant, which arose during a coinfection\ninvolving both subtypes. In a recent study reverse genetics was used\nto generate a laboratory reassortant of this type -- infection of\nferrets with this reassortant resulted in higher levels of virus and\nmore severe respiratory damage when compared to wild-type pH1N1 (2).\nThis case and the 5 reports of human infection with swine triple\nreassortant H3N2 in the United States since September 2010 highlight\nthe need for ongoing molecular and phenotypic surveillance for novel\ninfluenza strains (3).\n\nReferences\n----------\n1. Duncan C, Guthrie JL, Tijet N, et al: Analytical and clinical\nvalidation of novel real-time reverse transcriptase-polymerase chain\nreaction assays for the clinical detection of swine-origin H1N1\ninfluenza viruses. Diagn Microbiol Infect Dis. 2011; 69(2): 167-71\n[available at\nhttp://www.cnic.org.cn/pdf/2011-1-29/Analytical%20and%20clinical%20validation%20of%20novel%20.pdf].\n2. Schrauwen EJ, Herfst S, Chutinimitkul S, et al: Possible increased\npathogenicity of pandemic (H1N1) 2009 influenza virus upon\nreassortment. Emerg Infect Dis. 2011;17(2):200-8 [available at\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/eid/content/17/2/200.htm].\n3. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC). Update:\ninfluenza activity -- United States, 2010-11 influenza season, and\ncomposition of the 2011-12 influenza vaccine. MMWR-Morb Mortal Wkly\nRep. 2011; 60(21): 705-12 [available at\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm6021a5.htm].\n\nAuthors:\nR Eshaghi 1, SN Patel 1,2, A Li 1, D Grewal 3, D Kobasa 4, N Bastien\n4, Y Li 4, DE Low 1,2,5, and JB Gubbay 1,2,5\n1. Public Health Laboratory, Ontario Agency for Health Protection and\nPromotion (OAHPP), Canada.\n2. University of Toronto, Toronto, Ontario, Canada.\n3. Mount Sinai Hospital, Toronto, Ontario. Canada.\n\n--\nJonathan Gubbay\nMedical Microbiologist\nPublic Health Laboratory - Toronto\nOntario Agency for Health Protection and Promotion (OAHPP)\nToronto, Ontario, Canada\n<jonathan.gubbay@oahpp.ca>\n\n[ProMED-mail is indebted to Dr Gubbay and colleagues for drawing the\nattention of readers to the outcome of an investigation which has\nestablished that the coinfection of a child with pandemic H1N1 and\nseasonal H3N2 influenza viruses in the Toronto area was accompanied by\ngenome reassortment in the patient ( _in vivo_ reassortment). A\nrecombinant (reassortant) virus was recovered from the patient\npossessing the external protein genes of the H3N2 influenza virus and\nthe internal protein genes of the pandemic H1N1 influenza virus.\n\nDuring the 2010/2011 influenza epidemic season in North America both\nthe seasonal H3N2 and the pandemic H1N1 viruses have been prevalent\nand the occurrence of coinfection is not surprising, but this appears\nto be the 1st demonstration of _in vivo_ reassortment. Fortunately the\ninfected child has recovered and the recombinant (reassortant) virus\nwas not transmitted. It remains to be seen whether this or similar\nreassortants will appear in subsequent epidemics. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "Toronto, Ontario, Canada\n<jonathan.gubbay@oahpp.ca>\n\n[ProMED-mail is indebted to Dr Gubbay and colleagues for drawing the\nattention of readers to the outcome of an investigation which has\nestablished that the coinfection of a child with pandemic H1N1 and\nseasonal H3N2 influenza viruses in the Toronto area was accompanied by\ngenome reassortment in the patient ( _in vivo_ reassortment). This appears to be especially problematic\nwhen this virus does not appear to produce clinical findings much\ndifferent from seasonal influenza.\n\nGiven swine H3N2 has been circulating for years now, it would probably\nbe helpful to understand in this instance how these cases came to the\nattention of the PA Dept. of Agriculture. Was there an influenza\nsurveillance system already in place where virus samples were being\ncollected passively and sequenced? If so, this does not bode well for\nclinically-based recognition of 'unusual' morbidity versus mortality,\ngiven the number of areas in the country without such capabilities.\nThus, these warnings of swine H3N2/pandemic H1N1 recombinants have\nlimited practical value for the reporting base that public health\ndepends on: the clinicians.\n\nAs a nation (and indeed, the world), this places us in a 'fall-back'\nposition of waiting for an abrupt, unusual influx of morbidity into\nthe healthcare infrastructure, with the attendant occasional high\nmorbidity/mortality seen in the ICU setting. In such acute\npresentations, as we have seen with the pandemics of 1889, 1918, 1957,\n1968, other seasons of 'bad influenza (vaccine drift),' and the recent\npandemic of 2009, human recognition of such signature patterns is NOT\nbased on laboratory surveillance. Indeed, such bias prevented rapid\nrecognition of the 2009 pandemic influenza signature patterns in\nMexico in early 2009 and the surprised, reactive response observed.\nMinimization of surprise is essential for a well-considered response.\n\nWhat I am attempting to highlight here is the operational difference\nbetween laboratory surveillance that highlights a potential threat of\npublic health significance and event-based surveillance that\nhighlights the 'so what'/verification of significance. Finding a way\nto bridge different surveillance approaches that feed into a proactive\nwarning system remains an essential need for our country and, indeed,\nthe world.\n\n--\nJames M. Wilson V, MD\nExecutive Director\nPraecipio International\nWashington-Houston-Port au Prince\n<jim.wilson@praecipiointernational.org>\n\n[ProMED-mail welcomes the opportunity to communicate James Wilson's\nobservations and questions to a wider readership. This moderator does\nnot have information to contribute at this stage. Interested readers\nshould communicate directly with Dr. Wilson, and any outcomes may be\nposted. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "Thus, these warnings of swine H3N2/pandemic H1N1 recombinants have\nlimited practical value for the reporting base that public health\ndepends on: the clinicians.\n\nAs a nation (and indeed, the world), this places us in a 'fall-back'\nposition of waiting for an abrupt, unusual influx of morbidity into\nthe healthcare infrastructure, with the attendant occasional high\nmorbidity/mortality seen in the ICU setting. Maine Center for\nDisease Control director Dr Sheila Pinette says the federal CDC\n[Centers for Disease Control and Prevention] has confirmed the novel\nstrain. Pinette says the child was not hospitalized and is recovering\nfrom the illness.\n\nNo other human cases have turned up. The Maine CDC and Department of\nAgriculture are currently investigating exposures, Pinette says. She\nsays the type of influenza identified in the child is similar to other\ninfections that people have caught from swine and contains a genetic\ncomponent of the pandemic H1N1 virus that swept through Maine in\n2009.\n\nThe novel virus that sickened the child is known as the H3N2 virus and\nrarely infects humans, Pinette says. She says the virus is genetically\nsimilar to 4 previous cases found in the United States this year\n[2011], 3 of them in Pennsylvania and one in Indiana. All of those\nsickened in those incidents had been exposed to swine. Pinette says\nthe case that has turned up in Maine appears to be an isolated one,\nand there is no indication of any person-to-person transmission.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Background from 'Reported human infections with swine-origin\ninfluenza viruses (SOIV) in the United States since 2005,' (CDC\nwebsite, http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/soiv_cases.htm): As of 9\nSep 2011, 25 cases of human infection with swine origin influenza\nviruses have been reported in the United States. These are viruses\nthat normally infect pigs. Like human influenza viruses, there are\ndifferent subtypes and strains of swine-origin influenza viruses. The\nmain swine influenza viruses circulating in US pigs in recent years\nare swine triple reassortant (tr) H1N1 influenza virus, trH3N2 virus\nand trH1N2 virus. Of the 25 human cases reported since 2005, 12 have\nbeen trH1N1 viruses; 12 have been trH3N2 viruses and one has been a\ntrH1N2 virus. All 25 persons infected with swine viruses recovered\nfrom their illnesses. 18 cases occurred in children (persons 18 or\nyounger), and 7 cases occurred in adults. In 21 cases, direct or\nindirect exposure to swine prior to onset of illness has been\nidentified. Likely transmission of swine-origin influenza virus from\nclose contact with an infected person has been observed in\ninvestigations of human infections with swine-origin influenza A virus\nbut has not resulted in sustained human-to-human transmission.\n\nThe type of influenza identified in the Maine case is similar to more\nrecent rare human infections with a swine-origin H3N2 reassortant\nvirus, which contains a genetic component of the pandemic H1N1 virus.\nThe Maine virus is genetically similar to 4 previous cases identified\nin the United States this year (2011), 3 in Pennsylvania, and one in\nIndiana (see the ProMED-mail references below), all of which had\nexposure to swine. The child in Maine became ill in early October 2011\nwith symptoms similar to seasonal influenza including fever, cough,\nheadache, sore throat, and myalgia. Prior to illness, the child had\nexposure to swine, including time spent in a closed setting at an\nagricultural fair. Maine CDC and the Department of Agriculture are\ncurrently investigating exposures. Presently, this is an isolated\nevent, and Maine CDC has not confirmed any person-to-person\ntransmission.\n\nCumberland county in the state of Maine can be located in the\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map at:\nhttp://healthmap.org/r/1m1U. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Background from 'Reported human infections with swine-origin\ninfluenza viruses (SOIV) in the United States since 2005,' (CDC\nwebsite, http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/soiv_cases.htm): As of 9\nSep 2011, 25 cases of human infection with swine origin influenza\nviruses have been reported in the United States. "2011-09-06 17:54:46", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (52): (PENNSYLVANIA), SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2 REASSORTANT, 3 CASES\n**********************************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Mon 5 Sep 2011\nSource: PRNewswirw [edited]\nhttp://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/novel-influenza-a-cases-linked-to-county-fair-pennsylvanians-should-continue-flu-prevention-practices-and-report-flu-like-illness-129260413.html\n\n\n\nNovel Influenza-A Cases Linked to County Fair\n---------------------------------------------\nThe Pennsylvania Departments of Health and Agriculture today announced\n3 cases of a novel influenza A virus have been identified, and are now\nlinked to an agricultural fair in southwestern Pennsylvania.\n\nThe 1st individual to become ill, announced on Friday (2 Sep 2011),\nhas fully recovered from the illness. 2 other individuals, confirmed\nill over the weekend, are recovering. All 3 are children who reported\nattending the Washington County Agricultural Fair the week of 13-20\nAug 2011.\n\nThe cases in Pennsylvania are similar to previous, rare human\ninfections with swine-origin H3N2 viruses, but are unique in that they\ncontain a genetic component of the H1N1 virus.\n\nA continuing investigation, which is being jointly undertaken by the\nDepartments of Health and Agriculture, as well as the Centers for\nDisease Control and Prevention (CDC), has not yet uncovered how the\nillness was transmitted to the 3 individuals. However, no additional\nhuman infections with this virus have been identified to date.\n\nAnyone who attended the Washington County Fair and has flu-like\nsymptoms should contact their local health care provider or call the\nPennsylvania Departments of Health. Symptoms would be similar to that\nof seasonal influenza, and would include fever, lethargy (extreme\ntiredness), lack of appetite and coughing. Other influenza symptoms\nmay include a runny nose, sore throat, eye irritation, nausea,\nvomiting and diarrhea.\n\nThe Department of Health [DOH] and the CDC are conducting increased\nsurveillance and tracking in southwestern Pennsylvania, as well as\nsetting up informational booths about influenza at agricultural fairs,\nwhile Department of Agriculture is continuing with monitoring the\nhealth of animals at all exhibitions. The Department of Health\ncontinues to urge the public to take everyday precautions to prevent\nthe spread of respiratory viruses, while the Department of Agriculture\nreminds residents to remember to wash your hands after coming into\ncontact with animals at fairs and in other public venues.\n\n'We're not telling people to avoid public venues or fairs,' said\nPennsylvania DOH Secretary Dr. Eli Avila. 'But, until we complete our\ninvestigation, we want to make sure that the public is aware and is\ntaking the proper precautions to protect their health.'\n\nEveryday preventative actions against influenza include:\n\n-- Coughing or sneezing into a tissue, your sleeve or elbow (not your\nhands);\n\n-- Washing your hands often with soap and water for at least 20\nseconds, especially after you cough or sneeze, or using an\nalcohol-based hand cleaner;\n\n-- Keeping your hands away from your face -- don't touch your mouth,\nhands or eyes;\n\n-- Keeping frequently used surfaces clean; and\n\n-- Staying home from work, school, and social gatherings if you have\nflu-like and feverish symptoms to help prevent others from catching\nyour illness.\n\nFor more information, visit http://www.health.state.pa.us.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Mary Marshall\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[A total of 2 cases of febrile respiratory illness caused by\nswine-origin influenza A (H3N2) viruses were identified on 19 Aug 2011\nand 26 Aug 2011, and their characterisation has been described in the\nProMED-mail post archived as 'Influenza (51): swine-origin H3N2\nreassortant, children 20110902.2685.' One case (case A) was a boy aged\n<5 year resident in Indiana. The other (case B) was a girl aged <5\nyears, resident in Pennsylvania. No epidemiologic link between the 2\ncases had been identified, and although investigations were ongoing,\nno additional confirmed human infections with this virus has been\nreported.\n\nThe 3 cases described in the above report from the Pennsylvania\nDepartment of Health are presumed to be case B and 2 new cases. On as\nyet circumstantial grounds they are being linked to attendance at the\nsame agricultural fair, suggesting transmission of the infection from\nan animal source, rather than by person-to-person contact. Further\ninformation is awaited.\n\nThese viruses are similar to 8 other swine-origin influenza A (H3N2)\nviruses identified from previous human infections over the past 2\nyears, but are unique in that one of the 8 gene segments (matrix [M]\ngene) is from the 2009 influenza A (H1N1) virus. The acquisition of\nthe M gene in the 2 viruses so far characterised indicates that they\nare 'reassortants' because they contain genes of the swine-origin\ninfluenza A (H3N2) virus circulating in North American pigs since 1998\nand the 2009 influenza A (H1N1) virus that might have been transmitted\nto pigs from humans during the 2009 H1N1 pandemic.\n\nAlthough reassortment between swine influenza and 2009 influenza A\n(H1N1) viruses has been reported in pigs in the United States, this\nparticular genetic combination of swine influenza virus segments is\nunique and has not been reported previously in either swine or humans,\nbased on a review of influenza genomic sequences publicly available in\nGenBank. Analysis of data submitted to GenBank via the US Department\nof Agriculture (USDA) Swine Influenza Virus Surveillance Program\nsubsequent to this case identified 2 additional influenza A (H3N2)\nisolates from swine containing the M gene from the 2009 influenza A\n(H1N1) virus. [See also: MMWR Morb Mortal Wkly Rep 2011; 60(early\nrelease)\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm60e0902a1.htm?s_cid=mm60e0902a1_e&source=govdelivery.\n\n\nThe location of Washington County in the state of Pennsylvania can be\nfound in the maps at:\nhttp://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_County,_Pennsylvania. -\nMod.CP]", "summary": "Although reassortment between swine influenza and 2009 influenza A\n(H1N1) viruses has been reported in pigs in the United States, this\nparticular genetic combination of swine influenza virus segments is\nunique and has not been reported previously in either swine or humans,\nbased on a review of influenza genomic sequences publicly available in\nGenBank. The Pennsylvania Departments of Health and Agriculture today announced\n3 cases of a novel influenza A virus have been identified, and are now\nlinked to an agricultural fair in southwestern Pennsylvania. The cases in Pennsylvania are similar to previous, rare human\ninfections with swine-origin H3N2 viruses, but are unique in that they\ncontain a genetic component of the H1N1 virus. \n\nAnyone who attended the Washington County Fair and has flu-like\nsymptoms should contact their local health care provider or call the\n Symptoms would be similar to that\nof seasonal influenza, and would include fever, lethargy (extreme\ntiredness), lack of appetite and coughing.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2011-09-02 00:00:00", "2051-03-25 00:00:00", "2011-03-25 00:00:00", "2052-03-25 00:00:00", "2021-03-26 00:00:00", "2009-03-25 00:00:00", "1998-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Indiana", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Pennsylvania", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Washington County", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "855036", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Influenza (51): swine-origin H3N2 reassortant, children", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=855036", "date": "2011-09-02 17:40:38", "main_text": "INFLUENZA (51): SWINE-ORIGIN H3N2 REASSORTANT, CHILDREN\n*******************************************************\n\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri 2 Sep 2011\nSource: MMWR Morb Mortal Wkly Rep 2011; 60(early release): 1-4\n[abridged, edited]\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/mm60e0902a1.htm?s_cid=mm60e0902a1_e&source=govdelivery\n\n\nSwine-origin influenza A (H3N2) virus infection in 2 children\n-------------------------------------------------------------\nInfluenza A viruses are endemic in many animal species, including\nhumans, swine, and wild birds, and sporadic cases of transmission of\ninfluenza A viruses between humans and animals do occur, including\nhuman infections with avian-origin influenza A viruses (that is, H5N1\nand H7N7) and swine-origin influenza A viruses (that is, H1N1, H1N2,\nand H3N2) (1). Genetic analysis can distinguish animal origin\ninfluenza viruses from the seasonal human influenza viruses that\ncirculate widely and cause annual epidemics. This report describes 2\ncases of febrile respiratory illness caused by swine-origin influenza\nA (H3N2) viruses identified on 19 Aug 2011 and 26 Aug 2011, and the\ncurrent investigations. No epidemiologic link between the 2 cases has\nbeen identified, and although investigations are ongoing, no\nadditional confirmed human infections with this virus have been\ndetected. These viruses are similar to 8 other swine-origin influenza\nA (H3N2) viruses identified from previous human infections over the\npast 2 years, but are unique in that one of the 8 gene segments\n(matrix [M] gene) is from the 2009 influenza A (H1N1) virus. The\nacquisition of the M gene in these 2 swine-origin influenza A (H3N2)\nviruses indicates that they are 'reassortants' because they contain\ngenes of the swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) virus circulating in\nNorth American pigs since 1998 (2) and the 2009 influenza A (H1N1)\nvirus that might have been transmitted to pigs from humans during the\n2009 H1N1 pandemic. However, reassortments of the 2009 influenza A\n(H1N1) virus with other swine influenza A viruses have been reported\npreviously in swine (3). Clinicians who suspect influenza virus\ninfection in humans with recent exposure to swine should obtain a\nnasopharyngeal swab from the patient for timely diagnosis at a state\npublic health laboratory and consider empiric neuraminidase inhibitor\nantiviral treatment to quickly limit potential human transmission\n(4).\n\nCase reports\n------------\nPatient A -- On 17 Aug 2011, the United States Centers for Disease\nControl and Prevention [CDC] was notified by the Indiana State\nDepartment of Health Laboratories of a suspected case of swine-origin\ninfluenza A (H3N2) infection in a boy aged <5 years. The boy, who had\nreceived influenza vaccine in September 2010, experienced onset of\nfever, cough, shortness of breath, diarrhea, and sore throat on 23 Jul\n2011. He was brought to a local emergency department (ED) where a\nrespiratory specimen later tested positive for influenza A (H3). The\nboy was discharged home, but was not treated with influenza antiviral\nmedications. He has multiple chronic health conditions, returned to\nthe ED on 24 Jul 2011, and was hospitalized for treatment of those\nhealth problems, which had worsened. The boy was discharged home on 27\nJul 2011, and has since recovered from this illness. As part of\nroutine CDC-supported influenza surveillance, the respiratory specimen\ncollected on 24 Jul 2011, was forwarded to the Indiana State\nDepartment of Health Laboratories, where polymerase chain reaction\n(PCR) testing identified a suspect swine-origin influenza A (H3N2)\nvirus on 17 Aug 2011. The specimen was forwarded to CDC where the\nfindings were confirmed through genome sequencing on 19 Aug 2011.\n\nNo direct exposure to swine was identified for this child; however, a\ncaretaker reported direct contact with asymptomatic swine in the weeks\nbefore the boy's illness onset and provided care to the child 2 days\nbefore illness onset. No respiratory illness was identified in any of\nthe child's family or close contacts, the boy's caretaker, or in the\nfamily or contacts of the caretaker.\n\nPatient B -- On 24 Aug 2011, CDC was notified by the Pennsylvania\nDepartment of Health of a suspected case of swine-origin influenza A\n(H3N2) virus infection in a girl aged <5 years. The girl, who had\nreceived influenza vaccine in September 2010, experienced acute onset\nof fever, nonproductive cough, and lethargy on 20 Aug 2011. She was\nbrought to a local hospital ED where a nasopharyngeal swab tested\npositive for influenza A by rapid influenza diagnostic test. She was\nnot treated with influenza antiviral medications and was discharged\nhome the same day. The girl has completely recovered from this\nillness.\n\nA nasopharyngeal swab and nasal wash specimen were obtained at the ED\nand forwarded to the Pennsylvania State Department of Health Bureau of\nLaboratories for additional testing as part of routine CDC-supported\ninfluenza surveillance. On 29 Aug 2011, the state public health\nlaboratory identified a suspected swine-origin influenza A (H3N2)\nvirus by PCR testing, and both specimens were forwarded to CDC. On 26\nAug 2011, genome sequencing confirmed the virus as swine-origin\ninfluenza A (H3N2). On 16 Aug 2011, the girl was reported to have\nvisited an agricultural fair where she had direct exposure to swine\nand other animals. No additional illness in the girl's family or close\ncontacts has been identified, but illness in other fair attendees\ncontinues to be investigated. No additional confirmed swine-origin\ninfluenza virus infections have been identified thus far.\n\nEpidemiologic and laboratory investigations\n-------------------------------------------\nAs of 2 Sep 2011, no epidemiologic link between patients A and B had\nbeen identified, and no additional cases of confirmed infection with\nthe identified strain of swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) virus had\nbeen identified. Surveillance data from both states showed low levels\nof influenza activity at the time of both patients' illnesses. Case\nand contact investigations by the county and state human and animal\nhealth agencies in Indiana and Pennsylvania are ongoing, and enhanced\nsurveillance for additional human cases is being implemented in both\nstates.\n\nPreliminary genetic characterization of these 2 influenza viruses has\nidentified them as swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) viruses. Full\ngenome sequences have been posted to publicly available Web sites. The\nviruses are similar, but not identical to each other. 7 of the 8 gene\nsegments, including the hemagglutinin (HA) and neuraminidase (NA)\ngenes, are similar to those of swine H3N2 influenza viruses\ncirculating among U.S. pigs since 1998 (2) and previously identified\nin the 8 other sporadic cases of human infection with swine-origin\ninfluenza A (H3N2) viruses in the United States since 2009 [Additional\ninformation is available at\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/flu/weekly/pastreports.htm]. The one notable\ndifference from the viruses previously identified in human infections\nwith swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) virus is that these 2 viruses\nhave a matrix (M) gene acquired from the 2009 influenza A (H1N1)\nvirus, replacing the classical swine M gene present in the prior 8\nswine-origin influenza A (H3N2) virus infections in humans.\n\nAlthough reassortment between swine influenza and 2009 influenza A\n(H1N1) viruses has been reported in pigs in the United States (3),\nthis particular genetic combination of swine influenza virus segments\nis unique and has not been reported previously in either swine or\nhumans, based on a review of influenza genomic sequences publicly\navailable in GenBank. Analysis of data submitted to GenBank via the US\nDepartment of Agriculture (USDA) Swine Influenza Virus Surveillance\nProgram subsequent to this case identified 2 additional influenza A\n(H3N2) isolates from swine containing the M gene from the 2009\ninfluenza A (H1N1) virus. Genome sequencing is under way to completely\ncharacterize the genetic composition of these 2 swine influenza\nisolates. (USDA Agricultural Research Service and USDA Animal and\nPlant Health Inspection Service, unpublished data, 2011).\n\nThe viruses in these 2 patients are resistant to amantadine and\nrimantadine, but are susceptible to the neuraminidase inhibitor drugs\noseltamivir and zanamivir. Because these viruses carry a unique\ncombination of genes, no information currently is available regarding\nthe capacity of this virus to transmit efficiently in swine, humans,\nor between swine and humans.\n\nMMWR editorial note\n-------------------\n>From 2005 to 2007, CDC received reports of about one human infection\nwith a swine-origin influenza virus each year. In 2007, human\ninfection with a novel influenza A virus, including swine-origin\ninfluenza virus infections, became a nationally notifiable condition.\nSince that time, CDC has received about 3 to 5 reports a year of human\ninfections with swine-origin influenza viruses. The recent increase in\nreporting might be, in part, a result of increased influenza testing\ncapabilities in public health laboratories that allows for\nidentification of human and swine-origin influenza viruses, but\ngenetic changes in swine influenza viruses and other factors also\nmight be contributing to this increase (5--7). During December\n2005--November 2010, before the 2 cases described in this report, 21\ncases of human infection with swine-origin influenza were reported (12\ncases with swine-origin influenza A (H1N1) virus infection, 8 cases\nwith swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) virus infection, and one case\nwith swine-origin influenza A (H1N2) virus infection). 6 of these 21\ncases occurred in patients who reported direct exposure to pigs; 12\npatients reported being near pigs; human-to-human transmission was\nsuspected in 2 cases after epidemiologic investigations revealed no\nreported contact with swine in either case, but contact with ill\npersons who reported swine exposure was the suspected source of\ninfection; the exposure in one case was unknown (8) (CDC, unpublished\ndata; 2011). Although the vast majority of human infections with\nanimal influenza viruses do not result in human-to-human transmission\n(9,10), each case should be investigated fully to ascertain whether\nthese viruses are transmitted among humans and to limit further\nexposure of humans to infected animals, if infected animals are\nidentified.\n\nThe lack of known direct exposure to pigs in one of the 2 cases\ndescribed in this report suggests the possibility that limited\nhuman-to-human transmission of this influenza virus occurred. Likely\ntransmission of swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) virus from close\ncontact with an infected person has been observed in past\ninvestigations of human infections with swine-origin influenza A\nvirus, but has not resulted in sustained human-to-human transmission.\nPreliminary evidence from the investigation of the Indiana case shows\nno ongoing transmission. No influenza illness has been identified, but\nif additional chains of transmission are identified rapid intervention\nis warranted try to prevent further spread of the virus. Clinicians\nshould consider swine-origin influenza A virus infection as well as\nseasonal influenza virus infections in the differential diagnosis of\npatients with febrile respiratory illness who have been near pigs.\nClinicians who suspect influenza virus infection in humans with recent\nexposure to swine, should obtain a nasopharyngeal swab from the\npatient, place the swab in a viral transport medium, contact their\nstate or local health department to facilitate transport and timely\ndiagnosis at a state public health laboratory, and consider empiric\nneuraminidase inhibitor antiviral treatment (4).\n\n[Interested readers should access the original document at the source\nURL to view the references cited in the above text]\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This report describes 2 cases of febrile respiratory illness caused\nby swine-origin influenza A (H3N2) viruses identified on 19 Aug 2011\nand 26 Aug 2011. The viruses identified in these cases are unique in\nthat one of the 8 gene segments (matrix [M] gene) is derived from the\n2009 influenza A (H1N1) virus.\n\nNon-human influenza virus infections rarely result in human-to-human\ntransmission, but the implications of sustained ongoing transmission\nbetween humans is potentially severe; therefore, prompt and thorough\nidentification and investigation of these sporadic human infections\nwith non-human influenza viruses are needed to reduce the risk for\nsustained transmission. The 2 children had been vaccinated in the\nprevious year and had no known contact.\n\nAlthough reassortment between swine influenza and 2009 influenza A\n(H1N1) viruses has been reported in pigs in the United States\n[Duchatez MF, Hause B, Stigger-Rosser E, et al. Multiple reassortment\nbetween pandemic (H1N1) 2009 and endemic influenza viruses in pigs,\nUnited States. Emerg Infect Dis. 2011;17:1624-9], this particular\ngenetic combination of swine influenza virus segments is unique and\nhas not been reported previously in either swine or humans. There was\nno evidence of onward transmission of this particular reassortant in\nhumans\n\nTherefore clinicians should consider swine-origin influenza A virus\ninfection as well as seasonal influenza virus infections in the\ndifferential diagnosis of patients with febrile respiratory illness\nwho have been near pigs. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "6 of these 21\ncases occurred in patients who reported direct exposure to pigs; 12\npatients reported being near pigs; human-to-human transmission was\nsuspected in 2 cases after epidemiologic investigations revealed no\nreported contact with swine in either case, but contact with ill\npersons who reported swine exposure was the suspected source of\ninfection; the exposure in one case was unknown (8) (CDC, unpublished\ndata; 2011). No other cases have been\nidentified, but an investigation is underway. The influenza strain\nthat infected the child was identified as an H3N2 virus that commonly\ncirculates in pigs in North America. It is different from the\npandemic H1N1 virus, also of swine origin, that was first detected in\nthe United States in mid-April [2009]. [It presumably differs also\nfrom the current human seasonal H3N2 virus. - Mod.CP]\n\n'It is critical for people to understand that this H3N2 virus is not\nrelated to the pandemic H1N1 virus,' said Jason Eberhart-Phillips,\nKansas State Health Officer and Director of Health at the Kansas\nDepartment of Health and Environment (KDHE). 'This is not a mutation\nor a recombination of the pandemic strain, and it does not appear at\nthis time to be a threat to human health.' The child was likely\nexposed to the virus during the Riley County Fair in late July\n[2009], where the child had direct contact with pigs. The child later\ndeveloped influenza-like symptoms and sought medical care. The child\nhas fully recovered and no other family members have reported illness.\n\nThe KDHE is working closely with the Centers for Disease Control and\nPrevention (CDC), the U.S. Department of Agriculture (USDA), the\nRiley County Health Department and the Kansas Animal Health\nDepartment to investigate this case. The KDHE and the local health\ndepartment are working to determine if the Riley County Fair swine\nexhibitors, or their pigs, have been ill. Swine flu viruses do not\nnormally infect humans, but human infections occur from time to time.\nTypically the CDC has received reports of approximately one human\ninfection with a swine influenza virus each year. That number has\nrisen slightly in the past few years. The increased number of\nreported cases this year is likely the result of increased influenza\ntesting related to the H1N1 pandemic. So far this year, 14 cases of\nhuman infections with swine influenza viruses have been reported in\nthe United States. That number does not include the number of H1N1\ncases, as the H1N1 virus has not been detected in swine in the USA.\n\n'Most instances of human infection with animal influenza viruses,\nlike the swine H3N2 virus, do not result in human-to-human\ntransmission,' Dr. Eberhart-Phillips said. 'However, each case needs\nto be fully investigated to be sure that the viruses are not\nspreading among humans.' Most commonly, cases of human infection with\nswine influenza occur in people with direct exposure to pigs, he added.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[A map showing the location of Riley County in the NorthEast section\nof Kansas is available at:\n<http://geology.com/county-map/kansas.shtml>\n- CopyEd.EJP]\n................cp/ejp/dk\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The\nCenters for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) reported Friday [15\nJan 2010] that a child from Iowa became infected with a new swine flu\nvirus in September [2009], though the case didn't come to light until\nNovember. The unnamed boy didn't need to be hospitalized, and he\nrecovered fully from the illness. Testing later showed he'd been\ninfected with a swine influenza virus of the H3N2 subtype,\n*** different both from the pandemic H1N1 virus and from the seasonal\nH3N2 viruses that have been circulating in people for decades.*** [see\nProMED archives listed below]. Human cases of infection with swine\ninfluenza viruses happen from time to time. Often, though not always,\nthese infections are seen in people who work on pig farms or in\nproximity to swine herds.\n\nLast summer officials in Saskatchewan spotted a different swine H1N1\nvirus in 2 hog farm workers. As in the case of the boy in Iowa, that\nvirus seemed to stop spreading. 'While these cases are rare, they're\nnot unheard of,' Tom Skinner, a spokesman for the CDC, said from\nAtlanta, Georgia. 'We do detect them from time to time and it's\nimportant for us to investigate, which happened in this case. And\nluckily there wasn't any evidence of sustained person-to-person\ntransmission.'\n\nIt isn't clear how the boy became infected with the virus. He had no\nknown contact with pigs, an eerie echo of the emergence last spring of\nthe pandemic (H1N1) 2009 virus. [Pandemic] H1N1 was 1st spotted in 2\nchildren from California who had had no contact with pigs or with each\nother. 'It was a child, and yeah, very similar to the circumstances\nthat occurred last spring,' Skinner said of the Iowa case. He said the\nfact that the investigation turned up no other cases and that some\ntime has since elapsed suggests there isn't any ongoing spread. 'I\nthink if there was other transmission going on associated with this\ncase we would have picked it up and we haven't.'\n\nThere are a variety of influenza viruses circulating among pigs.\nAccording to the CDC, the 4 main types currently found are H1N1, H1N2,\nH3N1 and H3N2 viruses. Prior to the pandemic, a human case of swine\nflu in the United States was discovered every year or 2.
