[Epub ahead of print] [abstract,\nedited]\nhttp://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21715595\n\n\nInterspecies transmission of the canine influenza H3N2 virus to\ndomestic cats in South Korea, 2010\n--------------------------------------------------------------------\nAbstract\n--------\nIn the last 4 years, incidences of endemic or epidemic respiratory\ndiseases associated with canine influenza H3N2 virus in Asian dogs\nhave been reported in countries such as South Korea and China. Canine\nspecies were considered to be the new natural hosts for this virus.\nHowever, at the beginning of 2010, influenza-like respiratory signs,\nsuch as dyspnea, were also observed among cats as well as in dogs in\nan animal shelter located at Seoul, South Korea. The affected cats\nshowed 100 percent morbidity and 40 percent mortality.\n\nWe were able to isolate a virus from the lung specimen of a dead cat\nthat had suffered from the respiratory disease, in embryonated chicken\neggs. The 8 viral genes isolated were almost identical to those of the\ncanine influenza H3N2 virus suggesting interspecies transmission of\ncanine influenza H3N2 virus to the cat. Moreover, 3 domestic cats\ninfected with intranasal canine/Korea/GCVP01/07 (H3N2) all showed\nelevated rectal temperatures, nasal virus shedding, and severe\npulmonary lesions, such as suppurative bronchopneumonia. Our study for\nthe 1st time shows that cats are susceptible to canine influenza H3N2\ninfection, suggesting that cats may play an intermediate host role in\ntransmitting the H3N2 virus among feline and canine species, which\ncould lead to the endemic establishment of the virus in companion\nanimals.\n\nSuch a scenario raises a public health concern, as the possibility of\nthe emergence of new recombinant feline or canine influenza viruses in\ncompanion animals with the potential to act as zoonotic infection\ncannot be excluded.\n\n[Byline: Song DS, An DJ, Moon HJ, Yeom MJ, Jung HY, Jung WS, Park SJ,\nKim HK, Han SY, Oh JS, Park BK, Kim JK, Poo H, Webster RG, Jung K,\nKang BK. Korea Research Institute of Bioscience and Biotechnology]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Canine infections with influenza A viruses are currently known to be\ncaused by the serotypes H3N8 (USA, 2004), H3N2 (South Korea, 2007),\nHPAI H5N1 (Southeast Asia, 2006), and H1N1 (New York State, 2009). The\nspread of H3N2 from dogs to cats is a new development.\n\nRegarding possible zoonotic implications of the issue, subscribers\nare referred to the comprehensive reference document titled 'Avian\nInfluenza (Bird Flu): Implications for Human Disease,' updated to 16\nJun 2011, available at the CIDRAP (Center for Infectious Disease\nResearch and Policy, University of Minnesota) website via the general\nlink:\nhttp://www.cidrap.umn.edu/cidrap/content/influenza/avianflu/biofacts/avflu_human.html#_Agent\n\n(see also 20070519.1602).\n\nThe said document includes the following points which may be of\nrelevance to the point under discussion:\n\n1. The pandemic H3N2 strain of 1968-69 involved reassortment events\nbetween avian and human influenza strains.\n2. Human H3N2 influenza viruses are endemic in pigs in China.\n3. Seasonal influenza vaccine, which includes protection against H1N1\nand H3N2, may boost cross-subtype immunity against H5N1 influenza A\nviruses. This hypothesis is based on evidence suggesting that the N1\nantigen may be a target for both cellular and humoral cross-type\nimmunity.\n\nRecently, an investigation in Ontario, Canada, established that the\ncoinfection of a child with pandemic H1N1 and seasonal H3N2 influenza\nviruses was accompanied by genome reassortment in the patient (_in\nvivo_ reassortment); the infected child recovered and the recombinant\n(reassortant) virus was not transmitted; see 20110609.1749.\n\nThe importance of the 'One Health' approach and the clear need for\ncontinuous alertness by physicians and veterinarians alike, with\ninterchange of information, is once more underlined. Information of\npossible similar findings in other countries/species will be welcomed.\n- Mod.AS]\n\n[Seoul is shown on the HealthMap interactive map at\nhttp://healthmap.org/r/0*T6\n- Mod.JW]", "summary": "Our study for\nthe 1st time shows that cats are susceptible to canine influenza H3N2\ninfection, suggesting that cats may play an intermediate host role in\ntransmitting the H3N2 virus among feline and canine species, which\ncould lead to the endemic establishment of the virus in companion\nanimals. Test samples from 2\npatients submitted by Wisconsin and Pennsylvania have been confirmed at the\nCDC [Centers for Disease Control and Prevention] as positive for swine\norigin triple-reassortant (tr) H3N2 influenza viruses -- viruses that\nnormally infect pigs. While human infection with swine influenza viruses is\nrare, it can occur. This is most likely to occur when people are in close\nproximity to infected pigs, such as in pig barns and livestock exhibits\nhousing pigs at fairs.\n\nBoth of the patients with confirmed trH3N2 infection reported in FluView\nwere in the vicinity of live pigs. Dates of illness onset in the 2 patients\nare more than 6 weeks apart and the viruses from the 2 patients have some\ngenetic differences, confirming that these 2 cases are not linked. Ongoing\ninvestigations in both states have not shown any evidence of community\ntransmission of these viruses. The most likely scenario at this point is\nthat these are 2 isolated cases of human infection with swine influenza\nviruses that, while very rare, do occur from time to time. Both patients\nhave fully recovered from their illnesses; however, these 2 cases do\nunderscore the importance of human and animal surveillance for influenza.\n\nThese 2 cases reported in FluView bring the total number of human\ninfections with swine origin influenza viruses reported to CDC since 2005\nto 18. Previously, 3 of these reports had been swine origin A (H3N2)\nviruses. The Pennsylvania and Wisconsin cases bring the number of reports\nswine origin A (H3N2) infections in humans in the United States to 5. The\nviruses identified in Pennsylvania and Wisconsin are similar to viruses\nthat infected a patient in Iowa in September 2009, a patient in Kansas in\nAugust 2009 and a patient in Minnesota in May 2010.\n\nSwine Influenza (swine flu) is a respiratory disease of pigs caused by type\nA influenza viruses that regularly causes outbreaks of influenza in pigs.\nSwine flu viruses cause high levels of illness and low death rates in pigs.\nSwine influenza viruses may circulate among swine throughout the year, but\nmost outbreaks occur during the late fall and winter months similar to\noutbreaks in humans. There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that\nhave been isolated in pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. Most flu viruses\ncirculating in pigs are referred to as 'triple-reassortant' viruses because\nthese flu viruses contain genes from human, swine and avian influenza viruses.\n\nMost commonly, cases of human infection with swine-origin influenza viruses\noccur in people with direct exposure to pigs. The patient in Pennsylvania\nlives in an area where live pigs are farmed and the patient in Wisconsin\nbecame sick 2 days after attending a state fair where pigs were exhibited.\nIt's important to note that swine influenza viruses are not transmitted to\nhumans by food. You can not get swine influenza from eating pork or pork\nproducts. Eating properly handled and cooked pork and pork products is safe.\n\nIn the past, CDC received reports of approximately one human infection with\na swine influenza virus every one to 2 years, but in the past few years,\nabout 3 cases have been reported per year. Increased reporting of human\ninfections with swine influenza could be the result of increased influenza\ntesting capacity and capabilities in public health laboratories.\n\nThese trH3N2 viruses are different from the 2009 H1N1 virus that has been\ncirculating in the United States since late April 2009. They are also\ndifferent from human seasonal influenza A (H3N2) viruses that typically\ncirculate among people during the flu season. Swine trH3N2 viruses commonly\ncirculate in pigs in North America, but rarely infect humans. These viruses\nare different from the swine classical H1N1 or swine trH1N1 influenza\nviruses that also circulate in pigs in North America because they have H3N2\nsurface antigens. Tr H3N2 viruses first emerged in North American swine\nherds in the late 1990s. The H3 and N2 genes which 1st emerged in swine flu\nviruses originated from human seasonal H3N2 influenza viruses that\ncirculated globally among humans in the late 1990s.\n\nAlthough the vast majority of instances of human infection with animal\ninfluenza viruses do not result in human to human transmission, each case\nshould be fully investigated to be sure that such viruses are not spreading\namong humans and to limit further exposure of humans to infected animals if\ninfected animals are identified. Surveillance for both seasonal and novel\ninfluenza viruses is conducted by the CDC and its state and local health\npartners year round.\n\nFor more information about swine influenza, see:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/swineflu/>.\nWeekly US surveillance updates are published in FluView and posted at:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/flu/weekly/fluactivitysurv.htm>.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[There are 4 main influenza type A virus subtypes that have been isolated\nin pigs: H1N1, H1N2, H3N2, and H3N1. One is down with the H1N1 swine flu [influenza pandemic (H1N1)\n2009] virus, and the other is infected as influenza A virus subtype\nH3N2. As of Friday [31 Jul 2009], Taiwan has 6 cases of H1N1 and 2\nH3N2 cases. The [Taiwan] Center for Disease Control said that Taiwan\nis faced with threats of 2 flu types. The center said among the 6\nH1N1 cases, one has died. In addition, the center said that in the\nmonth of July [2009] alone, there had been 10 cases of H1N1 cluster\ninfections. There are at least 58 confirmed cases of H1N1.\n\nThe deputy director-general of the center, Lin Ting, said that since\nmost of the infected are men in their 30s, these men are being\nconsidered possibly for receiving the vaccines as well. Head of the\nphysical education department of the education ministry Wang\nJun-chuan said on Friday [31 Jul 2009] that schools have the right to\ndecide whether to stop classes if cases were to appear in schools.\n\n'We have been in touch with the [Taiwan] Centers of Disease Control.\nIf a school were to have one infected case, we have agreed that the\nschool can discuss with the county government and the health\ndepartment about whether to stop classes for up to 7 days. But if the\nsituation is serious, this can extended to 10 days. Secondly, if the\ncounty happens to have more than 2 cases in a school or a case of\ncluster infection, then the health department can decide for all\nschools in the district or nearby region to be shut down,' said Wang.\n\nHead of Taiwan's Health Department Yeh Jin-chuan will be traveling to\nHong Kong in August to discuss the pandemic with Hong Kong officials.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail>\n\n[For the interactive HealthMap/ProMED map for Taiwan, see:\n<http://healthmap.org/r/00DX> - CopyEd.EJP]\n\n******\n[2] China (Hong Kong S.A.R.) 'However, it has some genetic changes\ndistinguishing it from the old Brisbane strain,' he said. worldwide (11): coincident H3N2 variation", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=189739", "date": "2009-05-05 19:00:04", "main_text": "INFLUENZA A (H1N1) - WORLDWIDE (11): COINCIDENT H3N2 VARIATION\n**************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Tue 5 May 2009 12:08:07 -0700\nFrom: Danuta Skowronski\n<Danuta.Skowronski@bccdc.ca>\n\n\nRecent mutations away from the 2008-09 influenza vaccine strain among\nNorth American A/H3N2 virus coincident with emergence of novel A/H1N1\n--------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------\nThe BC [British Columbia] Centre for Disease Control (BCCDC) Virology\nLaboratory routinely sequences the hemagglutinin (HA) gene from a\nsample of influenza viruses submitted each season by community\nclinicians, hospitals and care facilities across the province of\nBritish Columbia, Canada.\n\nUntil mid-February 2009, amino acid sequences of the HA gene of H3\nviruses in BC were virtually identical to the vaccine strain [A/\nBrisbane/10/07 (H3N2)], with the exception of a Lys189Gln change at\nantigenic site B. In early March 2009, however, we detected\nadditional differences from the vaccine strain among BC viruses\ncollected from facility outbreak settings at antigenic site B to\ninclude change from the vaccine strain at Asp160Lys (as well as\nLys189Gln) and at antigenic site D at Val229Ala. These changes were\nseen only in viruses from care facility outbreaks and not from\ncommunity specimens submitted by our network of sentinel physicians.\n\nAfter the novel North American influenza A/H1N1 virus was reported\nfrom Mexico as well as California during the 4th week of April 2009,\nspecimens from returning travelers and others in BC with\ninfluenza-like illness were tested including influenza subtype\ndetermination and sequencing. Among approximately 900 respiratory\nspecimens submitted to the BCCDC laboratory between 24 Apr 2009 and 3\nMay 2009, the majority was negative for influenza; an equal number of\ninfluenza A detections were of the H1 or H3 subtype.\nWe have sequenced the HA gene of one of the H3 viruses from an ill\ntraveler returning from Mexico and find it shares the same amino acid\nchanges noted above.\n\nThese amino acid substitutions do not fulfill the criteria proposed\nby Cox as corresponding to meaningful antigenic drift (requiring at\nleast 2 amino acid substitutions at 2 or more defined antigenic sites\nA-E)[1] They may, however, signal important evolution in the HA gene.\nDuring late March and early April 2009 we reported an unexpected\nnumber of late-season care facility outbreaks due to H3 influenza. We\nshould thus remain vigilant for further H3N2 evolution and reduced\nvaccine relatedness since A/Brisbane/10/07(H3N2) has been retained as\nthe proposed vaccine component for both the 2009 southern and 2009-10\nnorthern hemisphere influenza seasons. Ongoing gene sequence analysis\nof H3 viruses from other countries would be informative. In BC, these\nH3 mutations arose sometime in early March 2009 and we observe at\nleast one returning traveler to have likely acquired illness due to\nthis virus in Mexico (specimen collected in BC 26 Apr 2009). We thus\nalso wonder to what extent the profile of influenza-like illness\ninitially reported from mid-March in Mexico may in part be attributed\nto this H3N2 variant in addition to emergence of the novel A/H1N1 virus.\n\nReference:\n----------\n[1] Cox NJ, Bender CA. The molecular epidemiology of influenza\nviruses. A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Tue 5 May 2009 12:08:07 -0700\n After the novel North American influenza A/H1N1 virus was reported\nfrom Mexico as well as California during the 4th week of April 2009,\nspecimens from returning travelers and others in BC with\ninfluenza-like illness were tested including influenza subtype\ndetermination and sequencing. We\nshould thus remain vigilant for further H3N2 evolution and reduced\nvaccine relatedness since A/Brisbane/10/07(H3N2) has been retained as\nthe proposed vaccine component for both the 2009 southern and 2009-10\nnorthern hemisphere influenza seasons. The BC [British Columbia] Centre for Disease Control (BCCDC) Virology\nLaboratory routinely sequences the hemagglutinin (HA) gene from a\nsample of influenza viruses submitted each season by community\nclinicians, hospitals and care facilities across the province of\nBritish Columbia, Canada. \nDanuta M Skowronski MD, MHSc, FRCPC, Tracy Chan BSc, Naveed Z Janjua\nMD, DrPH, Travis Hottes MSc, Annie Mak BSc, Martin Petric PhD, FCCM,\nMel Krajden MD, FRCPC, Patrick Tang MD, PhD, FRCPC, David Patrick MD,\nMHSc, FRCPC, Robert Brunham MD, FRCPC. In BC, these\nH3 mutations arose sometime in early March 2009 and we observe at\nleast one returning traveler to have likely acquired illness due to\nthis virus in Mexico (specimen collected in BC 26 Apr 2009). \n\nThese amino acid substitutions do not fulfill the criteria proposed\nby Cox as corresponding to meaningful antigenic drift (requiring at\nleast 2 amino acid substitutions at 2 or more defined antigenic sites\n More intriguing is\nthe observation that the same novel mutational changes were detected\namong viruses isolated from patients in care facilities in Canada as\nin a virus isolated from a traveler from Mexico. \n\nUntil mid-February 2009, amino acid sequences of the HA gene of H3\nviruses in BC were virtually identical to the vaccine strain [A/\n Recent mutations away from the 2008-09 influenza vaccine strain among\nNorth American A/H3N2 virus coincident with emergence of novel A/H1N1\n-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- We have sequenced the HA gene of one of the H3 viruses from an ill\ntraveler returning from Mexico and find it shares the same amino acid\nchanges noted above. These changes were\nseen only in viruses from care facility outbreaks and not from\ncommunity specimens submitted by our network of sentinel physicians. This\nis a matter that merits urgent investigation as it might help to\nexplain some of the unusual features of the current epidemic. During late March and early April 2009 we reported an unexpected\nnumber of late-season care facility outbreaks due to H3 influenza. The molecular epidemiology of influenza\nviruses. More people get sick\nduring flu seasons with mismatches. But a seasonal flu vaccine\nmismatch coinciding with a flu pandemic? That is no one's idea of a\ngood time. Dr. Allison McGeer groaned when she heard a new H3N2\nvariant is circulating in some parts of the Southern Hemisphere.\n'It's going to be a long winter. I know that already,' said McGeer,\nan influenza expert and head of infection control at Toronto's Mount\nSinai Hospital. 'It's not going to be pleasant because ... it's going\nto be one big long influenza season, from some time in September\nuntil next May.'\n\nThe new variant has been seen on a number of continents, though it\nstill remains a minority member of the H3N2 [strains], according to\nexperts at the World Health Organization (WHO) and the U.S. Centers\nfor Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in Atlanta. With the demands\nthe ongoing pandemic is placing on the WHO's laboratory network,\nresearchers haven't yet had time to study whether the new variant is\nmaking up a growing percentage of H3N2 viruses, said Dr. Nancy Cox,\ndirector of the CDC's influenza division. If they were, that would\nsuggest the variant was on its way to becoming the dominant H3N2\nvirus and a vaccine mismatch would be on the cards.\n\nFurther clouding the issue is the fact that labs around the world\nhaven't been submitting as many H3N2 viruses to the WHO network.\nThere are simply fewer of them around. 'We haven't had that many H3N2\nviruses to analyze because we've had such a flood of the novel H1N1\nviruses because they're predominating,' Cox said. Dr. Wenqing Zhang\nof the WHO's global influenza program said many Southern Hemisphere\ncountries are just coming into their regular flu seasons. The next\nmonth or so will give the world a clearer picture as to whether the\npandemic virus will crowd out the previous influenza A subtypes --\nmaking the composition of the seasonal vaccine less relevant -- or\nwhether one or both of the seasonal flu A virus [subtypes] will\ncontinue to circulate. And if H3N2 sticks around, the coming weeks\nwill also offer a sign as to whether this new variant is likely to\ncomplicate the upcoming Northern Hemisphere flu season.\n\n'While seemingly this variant H3 is emerging, we do not know to what\nextent it will be circulating,' Zhang said by e-mail from Geneva. Cox\nsaid the WHO collaborating labs will be heightening surveillance for\nseasonal flu viruses in the lead-up to the WHO's strain selection\nmeeting for the Southern Hemisphere seasonal vaccine, held in\nSeptember [2009]. One place they'll be checking is virus samples from\nnursing homes. H3N2 viruses prey on seniors and are behind many of\nthe frequent flu outbreaks seen in long-term care facilities. For\nsome reason, nursing home outbreaks are rarely caused by seasonal\nH1N1 viruses and so far the influenza pandemic (H1N1) 2009 virus has\nlargely spared that population too. 'If we start to see outbreaks in\nthose types of settings, this would increase our level of concern,'\nCox said. 'We haven't seen that to date.'\n\nBecause it takes months to make and ship flu vaccine, the viruses\ncovered by the seasonal flu shot have to be chosen long in advance.\nFor the Northern Hemisphere vaccine, experts like Cox gather at the\nWHO in February [2009] to assess the viruses circulating and make\ntheir best estimates of which will be the major disease sources in\nthe following winter. For the 2009-2010 winter they chose an H3N2\nvirus called A/Brisbane/10 first spotted in 2007. But within weeks of\nthe decision hints emerged that there was a new H3N2 variant, one\nsufficiently mutated ('drifted' in the language of flu) that it might\nbe able to evade the vaccine.\n\nResearchers in the influenza lab at the British Columbia Centre for\nDisease Control spotted it in early March [2009] as they were\nconducting late-season surveillance looking for just such viral\nevolution. In early May [2009], they reported the finding on\nProMed-mail, an electronic bulletin board and mailing list which\nmonitor infectious diseases outbreaks around the world [Influenza A\n(H1N1) - worldwide (11): coincident H3N2 variation 20090505.1679].\n\nDr. Danuta Skowronski, an influenza expert at the BCCDC, said she is\nexpecting this new variant to take over as the dominant H3N2 strain.\nAnd since the swine flu virus isn't infecting older adults much, it\ncould leave a niche for the new H3 variant. 'So it's hard to know. We\njust have to prepare for that possibility either way,' Skowronski\nsaid. 'The idea of having a mismatched drift strain circulating the\nsame year that we also have swine influenza [influenza pandemic\n(H1N1) 2009 virus infection] may mean that all segments of the\npopulation are affected by influenza one way or the other, whether\nit's the elderly with H3N2 and the young with H1N1.'\n\n[Byline: Helen Branswell]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[For an up-to-date detailed account of the prevention and control of\nseasonal influenza with vaccines, readers should consult the recently\npublished 'Recommendations of the Advisory Committee on Immunization\nPractices (ACIP), 2009.' MMWR Early Release Fri 24 July 2009 / 58\n(Early Release document);1-52, available at:\n<http://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/preview/mmwrhtml/rr58e0724a1.htm?s_cid=rr58e0724a1_e>.\n\n'This report updates the 2008 recommendations by CDC's Advisory\nCommittee on Immunization Practices (ACIP) regarding the use of\ninfluenza vaccine for the prevention and control of seasonal\ninfluenza (CDC. Prevention and control of influenza: recommendations\nof the Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices [ACIP]. MMWR\n2008;57[No. RR-7]). Information on vaccination issues related to the\nrecently identified novel influenza A H1N1 virus will be published\nlater in 2009. The 2009 seasonal influenza recommendations include\nnew and updated information. Highlights of the 2009 recommendations\ninclude 1) a recommendation that annual vaccination be administered\nto all children aged 6 months--18 years for the 2009-2010 influenza\nseason; 2) a recommendation that vaccines containing the 2009-2010\ntrivalent vaccine virus strains A/Brisbane/59/2007 (H1N1)-like,\nA/Brisbane/ 10/2007 (H3N2)-like, and B/Brisbane/60/2008-like antigens\nbe used; and 3) a notice that recommendations for influenza diagnosis\nand antiviral use will be published before the start of the 2009-2010\ninfluenza season. Vaccination efforts should begin as soon as vaccine\nis available and continue through the influenza season. Approximately\n83 percent of the United States population is specifically\nrecommended for annual vaccination against seasonal influenza;\nhowever, <40 percent of the U.S. population received the 2008-2009\ninfluenza vaccine.\n\nThese recommendations also include a summary of safety data for U.S.\nlicensed influenza vaccines. These recommendations and other\ninformation are available at CDC's influenza website\n(http://www.cdc.gov/flu ); any updates or supplements that might be\nrequired during the 2009-2010 influenza season also can be found at\nthis website. Vaccination and health-care providers should be alert\nto announcements of recommendation updates and should check the CDC\ninfluenza website periodically for additional information.'\n\nThe 2009 recommendations include 3 principal changes or updates, one\nof which is that: The 2009-2010 trivalent vaccine virus strains are\nA/ Brisbane/59/2007 (H1N1)-like, A/Brisbane/10/2007 (H3N2)-like, and\nB/ Brisbane 60/2008-like antigens. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "Highlights of the 2009 recommendations\ninclude 1) a recommendation that annual vaccination be administered\nto all children aged 6 months--18 years for the 2009-2010 influenza\nseason; 2) a recommendation that vaccines containing the 2009-2010\ntrivalent vaccine virus strains A/Brisbane/59/2007 (H1N1)-like,\n The next\nmonth or so will give the world a clearer picture as to whether the\npandemic virus will crowd out the previous influenza A subtypes --\nmaking the composition of the seasonal vaccine less relevant -- or\nwhether one or both of the seasonal flu A virus [subtypes] will\ncontinue to circulate. Pollack' <pollackmp@mindspring.com>\nSource: Reuters 11 Jan 2000 [edited]\n\nThe World Health Organisation (WHO) in Geneva says: 'This is a smart virus.\nThe virus is always ahead of us and whatever we do it will always be\naround,'' said Dr Daniel Lavanchy, WHO's coordinator of epidemic disease\nresponse.\nThe crisis has been caused by a virulent strain of the bug known as\nAustralian flu or Sydney H3N2. WHO experts correctly predicted the Sydney\nstrain was the one to fear this year. But Lavanchy complained: 'Immunisation\ncampaigns have not achieved their\ntargets in many, many countries. The target groups are not well covered with\na few exceptions like France. Europe and North America are in the grip of an\nepidemic.''\nMortality rates cannot be accurately calculated for up to two years after an\nepidemic. But officials stress the latest wave of flu is still a far cry\nfrom the 1918, 1957 and 1968 pandemics that killed millions around the\nglobe.\nUNITED STATES: THE Centre for Disease Control and Prevention said flu\nusually kills about 20 000 people a year in the United States. Although it\nmay seem that nearly every American has come down with a case of the flu,\nmedical experts stress that a serious epidemic is not underway there.\nBut, ironically, two newly approved flu drugs could be helping to spread\ninfluenza by inadvertently discouraging people from getting the flu vaccine.\nTamiflu is a pill made by Swiss drug company Roche Holdings AG (ROCZg.S),\nwhile Relenza, made by Glaxo Wellcome Plc (GLXO.L), is inhaled. Both reduce\nflu symptoms by one day on average.\nCHINA: the world's most populous nations, is reaping rich rewards from an\nambitious vaccination programme. A Ministry of Public Health spokeswoman\nsaid the campaign had produced 'good results.'' Many people, especially the\nold, lined up at Beijing\nhospitals for the flu shot.\n\nFLU SWEEPS EUROPE\nITALY: Two million Italians are reportedly suffering from flu with 250 000\nnew\ncases each week. This is the third consecutive week in which the number of\nflu sufferers in Italian hospitals has increased.\nSWITZERLAND: Georg Amstutz, a spokesman at the Swiss federal department of\nhealth, said the number of influenza cases in the Alpine state could be\nclassified as an\nepidemic and was approaching record numbers.\nNORWAY: Oslo's main Ullevaal hospital is hiring extra nurses from nearby\nDenmark\nand delaying non-emergency treatment because beds are filled with influenza\nsufferers.\nFRANCE: Antoine Flahault, research doctor at France's National Institute of\nHealth\nand Medical Research, INSERM, said: 'At this stage the epidemic most\nresembles 1989 that involved 5 million people in 13 weeks.'\nCZECH REPUBLIC: Many hospitals have banned visitors in an attempt to halt\nthe spread of the virus.\nSWEDEN: Professor Annika Linde from the Infectious Diseases Protection\nInstitute said the situation was very serious with hospitals overcrowded\nwith flu patients and battling staff shortages.\nUNITED KINGDOM: Health Secretary Alan Milburn told parliament 'There can\nhardly be a family in the land that has not been affected by the flu.'' One\nin 500 people are gripped by the virus and worse is to come.\n............................................jw\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. INFLUENZA A H3N2 - WORLD ROUNDUP\n************************\n But, ironically, two newly approved flu drugs could be helping to spread\ninfluenza by inadvertently discouraging people from getting the flu vaccine. The onset of illness in the\nindex case was on 17 Oct 2003. All patients serve on the same military\nbase. Outbreak 2 involved\nat least 38 patients, 29 of whom serve in a single company numbering some\n200 soldiers. The onset of illness in the index case was on 15 Oct 2003,\nwith most cases presenting at the base clinic over a 3-day period between\n21 Oct 2003 to 23 Oct 2003. Epidemiologic investigation has traced the onset of illness in\nthe index case to 15 Oct 2003. PCR performed on a nasopharyngeal swab\ncollected from the hospitalized patient showed the same strain as in\noutbreaks 1 and 2.\nThese 3 outbreaks were identified and investigated subsequent to reporting\nby primary care physicians on the involved military bases. A cooperative\neffort between clinicians, epidemiologists and laboratory specialists\nfacilitated rapid investigation, sample collection, laboratory diagnosis,\nviral typing, and public health intervention. Investigative efforts have\nbeen supplemented this year by the establishment of a laboratory-based\nfebrile respiratory illness surveillance system in collaboration with the\nvirology unit of the Hadassah Medical Organization.\nThese are the 1st influenza outbreaks to be reported in Israel this season.\nTo date, the Epidemiology Department of the Ministry of Health has reported\nno outbreaks of influenza in the civilian sector, although influenza A\n(H3N2) virus has been isolated from clinical specimens collected from\nsporadic cases of influenza-like illness in the cities of Elad and Jerusalem.\nIn the Israel Defense Force, the annual influenza vaccination campaign was\ninitiated on 19 Oct 2003, prior to detection of the outbreaks. 'There's not [a] threat to anybody or any other\nflocks,' Compton said on Tuesday [14 Sep 2004]. 'This was handled\nvery appropriately. Ag Forte, as a matter of prevention, quarantined\nthat flock.'\n\nAfter slaughter, the turkeys will be sold for meat, and that meat is\nsafe for consumption, said Rick Vanderspek, general manager and chief\noperating officer for Ag Forte. The meat will go into 'further\nprocessed' foods, such as turkey pot pies, or other foods using\nturkey as an ingredient.\n\nThis strain of the bird flu, as well as most others detected in North\nAmerica, are considered low pathogenic and are fairly mild compared\nwith the high-pathogenic flu in Asian countries. The Asian strains\nhave been devastating to that region's poultry industry and have\ncaused some human deaths.\n\nThe affected turkeys in Missouri were breed stock for Ag Forte, a\ncompany that produces turkey eggs, hatches those eggs, and sells the\nbaby turkeys, or poults, to other companies. The poultry companies\nthat buy the poults will then grow the birds until they are at\nslaughter weight, Vanderspek said.\n\nIt is common for Ag Forte to slaughter its breeder turkeys after\nthey've produced eggs for a certain amount of time. These turkeys,\nhowever, will be slaughtered earlier than usual because of the\ndetection of avian influenza.\n\nThis low-pathogenic avian flu strain is usually found among swine.\nLow pathogenic viruses cause less severe or no illness when they\ninfect poultry.\n\nAg Forte had some signs of the disease in its birds, before a routine\ntest for the virus detected it. 'We check monthly for the virus. We\ntest in our own labs and submit it to the state,' Vanderspek said.\n'Before that, we'd noticed our egg production wasn't what we\nexpected. That was a warning sign.'\n\nAg Forte owns the farm and the turkeys where the flu was detected.\n\nFor at least 30 days after the detection of avian influenza, Lawrence\nCounty, and counties adjacent to it, will not be able to export\npoultry to Russia, Vanderspek said. 'Russian export laws say that if\nany avian flu shows up, that state cannot export to Russia,' he said.\nIn this case, the ban is limited to Lawrence and adjacent counties.\n\nThe detection of this low pathogenic flu is not expected to further\naffect southwest Missouri's poultry industry, Compton said.\n\nAg Forte will lose some income as a result of the early slaughter of\nthe breeder turkeys, which were capable of producing eggs for several\nmore weeks. 'We'll have some financial impact, though it won't be\ndevastating,' Vanderspek said.\n\nAg Forte has about 250 000 breeding birds in its total operation.\n\n[Byline: Karen E. The most likely source of the virus is a swine herd in\nthe area, he said.\nThe flock was late in its laying cycle when one house experienced a\ndrop in egg production. The flock processed shortly thereafter.\nOn 14 Jan 2005, the National Veterinary Services Laboratory confirmed\nthat the virus isolated from the flock at Rollins laboratory was a\nsubtype H3N2 influenza virus. This diagnosis has significance for\nthose that export to the Russian Federation, said state veterinary\nofficials. The export agreements made with Russia include the\nrequirement to report all subtypes of influenza diagnosed in\ncommercial poultry.\nThe isolation of H3N2 influenza from North Carolina poultry will be\nreported by USDA to Russia. This will change North Carolina's Avian\nInfluenza status as it relates to Russian exports from A to B for the\nnext 30 days. After 30 days has passed the status will change to C.\nIt will be an additional 5 months before the status returns to A.\nWhile in status B, processing plants located in the index county and\nadjacent counties will not be able to export to Russia. The counties\naffected include Sampson, Harnett, Johnston, Wayne, Duplin, Pender,\nBladen and Cumberland.\nAll other processing plants in the state exporting to Russia will\nneed to test 15 birds per flock for the virus at slaughter during the\n30-day period if they wish to export to Russia. If the plant has no\nintention of exporting product produced during the 30-day period they\ndo not need to test.\nWhile in status C, processing plants located in the noted counties\ncan resume exports to Russia if they test 15 birds per flock at\nslaughter. Processing plants located outside of the index county and\nadjacent counties can discontinue testing for avian influenza.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[H3N2 is considered a low-pathogenicity variety. The export agreements made with Russia include the\nrequirement to report all subtypes of influenza diagnosed in\ncommercial poultry.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2004-02-08 00:00:00", "2005-01-14 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Russia", "country": "Russia"}, {"location": "North Carolina 's", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "North Carolina", "country": "Puerto Rico"}, {"location": "Duplin", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Sampson County", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "the Russian Federation", "country": "Uruguay"}, {"location": "Cumberland", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2206764", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Influenza A (H3N2), variant strain", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2206764", "date": "2004-10-24 23:50:00", "main_text": "INFLUENZA A (H3N2), VARIANT STRAIN\n*********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Sun 24 Oct 2004\nFrom: Jonathan Nash <jnash@qis.net>\nSource: San Francisco Chronicle, Sun 24 Oct 2004 [edited]\n<http://www.sfgate.com/cgi-bin/article.cgi?file=/c/a/2004/10/24/FLU.TMP>\n\nNorthern hemisphere: risk of A/Wellington/1/2004(H3N2)-like virus\n-----------------------------------------------\nAs Americans scramble for scarce doses of flu vaccine in hopes of warding\noff the respiratory bug this winter [2004-2005], the wily influenza virus\nmay have other plans. Through a natural process known as antigenic drift, a\nnew strain of influenza that can diminish the effectiveness of today's\nvaccine is already emerging on the far side of the world. The emergent\nstrain raising questions now is known as A/Wellington, named after the New\nZealand city where it was 1st detected. 'The flu season has been late this\nyear [2004], and it seems some people who have been vaccinated have been\nhit by this changing strain,' said New Zealand Health Ministry flu chief\nDr. Lance Jennings.\nLike weather forecasters watching tropical storms, epidemiologists for the\nWorld Health Organization track the ever-evolving strains of influenza. The\npredominant flu virus around the globe right now is one called A/Fujian,\nand the vaccine Americans are seeking today is a perfect match for it. But,\nA/Wellington is gaining ground. Tests suggest that 43 percent of recent New\nZealand flu cases spring from the new strain, or variants of it.\nA/Wellington has even turned up about as far from the South Pacific as is\ngeographically possible: in Norway.\nThe late season surge of A/Wellington was so worrying that the WHO, on 8\nOct 2004, recommended that next year's [2005] flu vaccine for the Southern\nHemisphere, which is shipped in March, be reformulated to protect against\nit. Dr. Nancy Cox, director of the Influenza Branch at the Centers for\nDisease Control and Prevention, said there is no way of knowing whether the\nA/Wellington strain will establish a beachhead in the United States this\nwinter. 'Influenza viruses are inherently unpredictable,' she said in a\ntelephone interview.\nOne reason for concern is that laboratory animal tests suggest that the\ncurrent vaccine -- which targets A/Fujian -- is about 2/3rds less effective\nin stirring antibodies against A/Wellington than it is against the targeted\nstrain. That does not mean, Cox emphasized, that the current flu shot would\nbe 2/3rds less effective in actual use, should the A/Wellington strain turn\nup in the United States. People who regularly receive flu shots, she said,\nmay have higher levels of antibody protection against influenza than the\nlaboratory animals. 'We would not anticipate that the Wellington strain\nwill cause an enormous problem,' she said.\nDoris Bucher, a flu vaccine expert at New York Medical College in Valhalla,\nN.Y., said the animal tests on A/Wellington are actually encouraging,\nbecause they show that antibodies stirred up by today's vaccine will\ninhibit the new strain. 'If Wellington rushed in and took over, people\nwould have good protection with the current vaccine,' she said. Bucher's\nlab specializes in developing strains of influenza that grow well in eggs\nand hence serve as the basis for each year's flu vaccine made from eggs.\nThe current flu vaccine is made from a strain developed by her laboratory,\nand she is working right now on an A/Wellington-like strain for\nmanufacturers who will make next year's [2005] vaccines.\nWhen flu vaccines are well-matched to the prevailing flu strains, the shots\ncan prevent flu in 70 to 90 percent of vaccinated adults, according to the\nCDC. Well-matched shots may prevent flu in only 30 to 40 percent of nursing\nhome residents, but they can reduce the death rate from influenza and\npneumonia in that population by 80 percent. American manufacturers need a\n9-month lead to produce flu vaccine. That requires flu forecasters to\npredict in February what the prevailing flu strain will be November.\nIn February 2003, the Food and Drug Administration's Vaccines and Related\nBiological Products Advisory Committee selected a strain called A/Panama as\nthe target. Another strain, known as A/Fujian, was starting to emerge on\nthe World Health Organization's flu radar, but planners feared there was\nnot enough time to develop a vaccine for it. Influenza struck the United\nStates early and hard last year [2003]. The 1st cases turned up in early\nOctober 2003 in Texas, and soon anxious Americans were clamoring for\nvaccine, a foreshadowing of this year's [2004] flu shot frenzy.\nA near-record 83 million eventually had a flu shot last fall [2003]. But,\nthe predominant flu strain was not A/Panama but A/Fujian, and the vaccine\nwas only partially effective. A Colorado study for the CDC concluded last\nyear's flu shots were 52 percent effective in protecting healthy adults\nagainst flu and 38 percent effective in preventing flu among those with\nhealth conditions putting them at higher risk.\nBucher said it is reassuring that tests are showing there is less of a\nmismatch between the current flu vaccine and A/Wellington than there was\nlast year [2003] between A/Panama and A/Fujian. 'It's nothing like the\nsituation we had last year,'' she said. The issue, of course, will be\nacademic if the A/Fujian strain predominates again this year, as it did in\nthe Southern Hemisphere during most of the winter season that has now ended\nthere. Those 61 million Americans who find the vaccine will be protected.\nThere is also some evidence that the A/Fujian strain, having swept across\nthe globe last year [2003], may have lost its punch. Despite the late\nemergence of the new flu strain, influenza was unusually mild throughout\nthe Southern Hemisphere from May through October 2004. One reason may be\nthat the human population, vaccinated or not, developed its own natural\nresistance to A/Fujian when it came through last winter [2003]. 'There are\na number of factors that determine whether you will have a good, medium, or\nbad flu year, and clearly a major factor is whether the predominant strain\nhappens to be the same as the one the previous year,'' said Dr. Anthony\nFauci, director of the National Institute of Allergy and Infectious\nDiseases. So far, there have been only isolated cases of flu detected in\nthe United States at the start of this year's flu season, and all have\nturned out to be A/Fujian. 'Things are looking pretty good, in that\nregard,'' Fauci said. 'But, that doesn't mean we won't get into trouble in\nNovember or December [2004].'\n[Byline: Sabin Russell]\n--\nJonathan Nash\n<jnash@qis.net>\n[The WHO Recommendations for Influenza Vaccine Composition for the Northern\nhemisphere for 2004-2005 (the current vaccine) are the following:\n(1) an A/New Caledonia/20/99(H1N1)-like virus\n(2) an A/Fujian/411/2002(H3N2)-like virus a\n(3) a B/Shanghai/361/2002-like virus b\na: The currently used vaccine virus is A/Wyoming/3/2003. A\n/Kumamoto/102/2002 is also available as a vaccine virus.\nb: Candidate vaccine viruses include B/Shanghai/361/2002 and\nB/Jilin/20/2003 which is a B/Shanghai/361/2002-like virus.\nFor more detailed information, please see: Recommended composition of\ninfluenza virus vaccines for use in the 2004-2005 influenza season, Weekly\nEpidemiological Record, 27 Feb 2004.\n<http://www.who.int/csr/disease/influenza/vaccinerecommendations1/en/index1.\nhtml>\nThe WHO Recommendations for Influenza Vaccine Composition for the southern\nhemisphere for 2005 (southern hemisphere winter) are the following:\n(1) an A/New Caledonia/20/99(H1N1)-like virus;\n(2) an A/Wellington/1/2004(H3N2)-like virus;\n(3) a B/Shanghai/361/2002-like virus a\na: Currently used vaccine viruses include B/Shanghai/361/2002,\nB/Jilin/20/2003 and B/Jiangsu/10/2003\nFor more detailed information, please see: Recommended composition of\ninfluenza virus vaccines for use in the 2005 influenza season, Weekly\nEpidemiological Record, 8 Oct 2004.\n<http://www.who.int/csr/disease/influenza/vaccinerecommendations1/en/index.h\ntml>.\n- Mod.CP]", "summary": "The WHO Recommendations for Influenza Vaccine Composition for the southern\nhemisphere for 2005 (southern hemisphere winter) are the following:\n(1) an A/New Caledonia/20/99(H1N1)-like virus;\n(2) an A/Wellington/1/2004(H3N2)-like virus;\n(3) a B/Shanghai/361/2002-like virus a\na: Currently used vaccine viruses include B/Shanghai/361/2002,\nB/Jilin/20/2003 and B/Jiangsu/10/2003\n [The WHO Recommendations for Influenza Vaccine Composition for the Northern\nhemisphere for 2004-2005 (the current vaccine) are the following:\n(1) an A/New Caledonia/20/99(H1N1)-like virus\n(2) an A/Fujian/411/2002(H3N2)-like virus a\n(3) a B/Shanghai/361/2002-like virus b\na: The currently used vaccine virus is A/Wyoming/3/2003. A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n \nDoris Bucher, a flu vaccine expert at New York Medical College in Valhalla,\nN.Y., said the animal tests on A/Wellington are actually encouraging,\nbecause they show that antibodies stirred up by today's vaccine will\ninhibit the new strain. ' A Colorado study for the CDC concluded last\nyear's flu shots were 52 percent effective in protecting healthy adults\nagainst flu and 38 percent effective in preventing flu among those with\nhealth conditions putting them at higher risk. The flu season has been late this\nyear [2004], and it seems some people who have been vaccinated have been\nhit by this changing strain,' said New Zealand Health Ministry flu chief\n [Byline: Sabin Russell]\n--\nJonathan Nash\n<jnash@qis.net>\n For more detailed information, please see: Recommended composition of\ninfluenza virus vaccines for use in the 2005 influenza season, Weekly\nEpidemiological Record, 8 Oct 2004.\n One reason for concern is that laboratory animal tests suggest that the\ncurrent vaccine -- which targets A/Fujian -- is about 2/3rds less effective\nin stirring antibodies against A/Wellington than it is against the targeted\nstrain. The issue, of course, will be\nacademic if the A/Fujian strain predominates again this year, as it did in\nthe Southern Hemisphere during most of the winter season that has now ended\nthere. Dr. Nancy Cox, director of the Influenza Branch at the Centers for\nDisease Control and Prevention, said there is no way of knowing whether the\nA/Wellington strain will establish a beachhead in the United States this\nwinter. ' The current flu vaccine is made from a strain developed by her laboratory,\nand she is working right now on an A/Wellington-like strain for\nmanufacturers who will make next year's [2005] vaccines. When flu vaccines are well-matched to the prevailing flu strains, the shots\ncan prevent flu in 70 to 90 percent of vaccinated adults, according to the\nCDC. The 1st cases turned up in early\nOctober 2003 in Texas, and soon anxious Americans were clamoring for\nvaccine, a foreshadowing of this year's [2004] flu shot frenzy. The late season surge of A/Wellington was so worrying that the WHO, on 8\nOct 2004, recommended that next year's [2005] flu vaccine for the Southern\n Bucher said it is reassuring that tests are showing there is less of a\nmismatch between the current flu vaccine and A/Wellington than there was\nlast year [2003] between A/Panama and A/Fujian. ' So far, there have been only isolated cases of flu detected in\nthe United States at the start of this year's flu season, and all have\nturned out to be A/Fujian. ' For more detailed information, please see: Recommended composition of\ninfluenza virus vaccines for use in the 2004-2005 influenza season, Weekly\nEpidemiological Record, 27 Feb 2004. Bucher's\nlab specializes in developing strains of influenza that grow well in eggs\nand hence serve as the basis for each year's flu vaccine made from eggs. Despite the late\nemergence of the new flu strain, influenza was unusually mild throughout\nthe Southern Hemisphere from May through October 2004. Another strain, known as A/Fujian, was starting to emerge on\nthe World Health Organization's flu radar, but planners feared there was\nnot enough time to develop a vaccine for it. The\npredominant flu virus around the globe right now is one called A/Fujian,\nand the vaccine Americans are seeking today is a perfect match for it. But,\nthe predominant flu strain was not A/Panama but A/Fujian, and the vaccine\nwas only partially effective.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2003-03-25 00:00:00", "2004-10-08 00:00:00", "2021-02-25 00:00:00", "2004-03-25 00:00:00", "2004-02-27 00:00:00", "2021-03-25 00:00:00", "2021-11-25 00:00:00", "2005-03-25 00:00:00", "2003-02-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Colorado", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Norway", "country": "Norway"}, {"location": "the United \n States", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "the United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Texas", "country": "United States"}, {"location": "Wellington", "country": "New Zealand"}, {"location": "N.Y.", "country": "Papua New Guinea"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "141112", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Influenza, seasonal, H3N2 - UK", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=141112", "date": "2008-12-31 19:00:41", "main_text": "INFLUENZA, SEASONAL, H3N2 - UNITED KINGDOM\n******************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Wed 31 Dec 2008\nSource: Mail online [abbreviated and edited]\n<http://www.dailymail.co.uk/health/article-1102485/Killer-Australian-flu-strain-set-spread-UK-experts-warn.html>\n\n\n'Killer' Australian flu strain set to spread across UK, experts warn\n--------------------------------------------------------------------\nExperts are warning that the current outbreaks of\nflu are expected to intensify in the new year\n[2009] as the Australian strain that can be fatal\nspreads across the UK. The current flu epidemic\n-- already on course to be the worst for 8 years\n-- will get worse as the infection usually peaks\nbetween January and March, they warn.\n\nThe European Influenza Surveillance Scheme (EISS)\nsays the H3N2 Brisbane 10 flu strain that caused\na severe epidemic and killed 6 children in\nAustralia is spreading -- with the UK, Ireland,\nBulgaria and Spain experiencing it at 'medium\nintensity.' The UK Health Protection Agency and\nthe Royal College of GPs have reported a sharp\nrise in the number of flu-like illnesses over the\npast 2 weeks, with pensioners being the worst-hit.\n\nThe sharp rise left the NHS struggling to cope\nover Christmas. Thousands of GPs' surgeries close\nover this period, leaving sufferers with nowhere\nto go for help except accident and emergency\nwards. But hospitals are already under more\npressure than usual because of outbreaks of the\nwinter vomiting bug norovirus. Latest figures\nshow that among the over-65s, 45 in every 100 000\npeople now have flu, up from just 18 a week ago.\nThough it is still the 15 to 44 age group who are\nreporting the most cases, with a rate of 80 per\n100 000, it is the elderly who are most likely to die from flu.\n\nThis year's [2008] outbreak, blamed on an\nAustralian strain of the virus called Brisbane\nH3N2, is well on course to be the worst since\n1999, when 22 000 people died. However, an\noutbreak does not officially become an epidemic\nuntil the rate is higher than 200 per 100 000.\nThe last time this happened was in 1989.\n\nProfessor Steve Field, chairman of the Royal\nCollege of GPs, said: 'Older age groups are now\nshowing increases, and they have the most serious\nconsequences -- the extra load on the lungs,\npossible viral pneumonia and the increased risk\nof contracting a 2nd bacterial infection. The NHS\nis coping remarkably well in the circumstances,\nalthough there are some very desperate problems\nin the West Midlands. The outbreak is affecting\nyoung adults and many of these are health service\nworkers. When added together, there are a lot of\nproblems. The influenza vaccination programme has\nbeen good in some areas but patchy in others. It\nis these areas where the NHS will take a big hit.'\n\nJohn Heyworth, incoming president of the College\nof Emergency Medicine, said most people with flu\nwould not need to see a health professional and\nwould be fine within 2 or 3 days. But he added:\n'The bottom line is that if people can't access\ncare in the community and need to see someone,\nthey should come to accident and emergency\nbecause we don't want bad things to happen. If\nthere is any doubt, we are happy to help.'\n\nThe Health Protection Agency said: 'Although it\nis too early to predict how much further flu will\nincrease, the agency's advice is to be alert to\nsymptoms. These can include a headache, fever,\ncough, sore throat, and aching muscles and\njoints. For the majority of people, flu is an\nunpleasant, but not life-threatening, illness.\nBut it can be dangerous for certain groups, such\nas the elderly or those with heart problems,\ndiabetes or asthma and those who are immuno-compromised.'\n\nDavid Nicholson, NHS Chief Executive, said:\n'Thanks to the extra resources, staff and\nhospital capacity we've put in over the last 10\nyears, along with better planning, the NHS is well prepared for winter.'\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nPoMED-mail\n\n\n[To put this press report in perspective, current statements on\ninfluenza surveillance in Europe are appended below.\n\no From Health Protection Report, Vol.2, no 51, 19\nDec 2008 (<http://www.hpa.org.uk/hpr/>):\n\n'Influenza activity has started earlier this\nseason compared to recent years. Epidemiological,\nclinical and virological indicators show that\ninfluenza viruses are circulating in the\ncommunity at moderate levels. Infuenza is now\nwidespread across parts of the UK and continues to increase.\n\nThe percentage of ILI (Influenza-like illnesses)\ncases with laboratory confirmed influenza through\nGP sentinel schemes in England and Wales exceeded\nthe threshold of 30 percent in week 48 and is\ncurrently at 50.6 percent. A total of 403 samples\nhave tested positive for influenza in the\nRespiratory Virus Unit (RVU) influenza reference\nlaboratory at the HPA's Centre for Infections\n(CfI) since week 40. The vast majority of\ncirculating influenza has been influenza A(H3)\nwith 359 (89.1 percent) isolates. A further 34\n(8.4 percent) influenza A(H1) and 10 (2.5\npercent) influenza B isolates have been detected.\nAll 42 A(H3) isolates tested since week 40/08\nhave been sensitive to oseltamivir and zanamivir\nbut resistant to amantadine. Of the 28 A(H1)\nspecimens tested, 27 are resistant to oseltamivir\nbut sensitive to zanamivir and amantadine.\nViruses characterised at RVU show that the\ncurrent seasonal influenza vaccine is well\nmatched to circulating strains and should provide\ngood protection. The number of influenza positive\ndetections from other NHS and HPA laboratories in\nEngland and Wales has increased in the past few\nweeks and almost doubled from week 49 to week 50.\nSimilar patterns have been seen in Scotland and Northern Ireland.'\n\no From the EISS Weekly Electronic bulletin, Week 51, 25 Dec 2008\n(<http://www.eiss.org/cgi-files/bulletin_v2.cgi>):\n\n'Epidemiological situation -- week 51/2008: For\nthe intensity indicator, the national network\nlevels of influenza-like illness (ILI) and/or\nacute respiratory infection (ARI) were high in\nPortugal and medium in Bulgaria, Ireland, Spain\nand the UK (England and Northern Ireland). The\nremaining 16 countries providing data reported\nlow intensity with increasing trends in Denmark,\nFrance, Germany, Italy, Sweden and Switzerland.\nFor the geographical spread indicator, widespread\ninfluenza activity occurred in Portugal and the\nUK (England and Northern Ireland), while regional\nactivity was reported in Spain and the UK (Wales)\nand local activity in France and Switzerland.\nSporadic activity was observed in 10 countries\nwhile no activity was reported in Estonia,\nGreece, Hungary, Poland and Romania. Definitions\nfor the epidemiological indicators can be found here.\n\nCumulative virological situation \u00ad 2008-2009\nseason (weeks 40-51/2008): Of 2026 virus\ndetections (sentinel and non-sentinel) since week\n40/2008, 1929 were type A (838 subtype H3, 55\nsubtype H1 and 1036 not subtyped) and 97 were type B.\n\nBased on the antigenic and/or genetic\ncharacterisation of 234 influenza viruses, 206\nwere reported as A/Brisbane/10/2007 (H3N2)- like,\n16 as A/Brisbane/59/2007 (H1N1)-like, 8 as\nB/Malaysia/ 2506/2004-like (B/Victoria/2/87\nlineage) and 4 as B/Florida/4/2006- like (B/Yamagata/16/88 lineage).\n\n42 A(H3N2) viruses have been tested for antiviral\nsusceptibility so far this season; all those\ntested were sensitive to neuraminidase inhibitors\n(42 for oseltamivir, 40 for zanamivir) and\nresistant to the M2 inhibitor amantadine (40). Of\n29 A(H1N1) viruses tested, all were sensitive to\nzanamivir and 28 were resistant to oseltamivir,\nwhilst all of the 19 tested against amantadine\nwere sensitive. Both B viruses tested have been\nsensitive to neuraminidase inhibitors (one was\ntested against both zanamivir and oseltamivir and\none against oseltamivir only). But for 2\noseltamivir resistant A(H1N1) viruses from\nNorway, all other viruses tested have been from the UK.'\n\nWhile the situation in the UK is becoming\nserious, there seems little justification for the\nexpression 'killer' employed by the above press report. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "\n\nCumulative virological situation \u00ad 2008-2009\nseason (weeks 40-51/2008): Of 2026 virus\ndetections (sentinel and non-sentinel) since week\n40/2008, 1929 were type A (838 subtype H3, 55\nsubtype H1 and 1036 not subtyped) and 97 were type B.\n\nBased on the antigenic and/or genetic\ncharacterisation of 234 influenza viruses, 206\nwere reported as A/Brisbane/10/2007 (H3N2)- like,\n16 as A/Brisbane/59/2007 (H1N1)-like, 8 as\nB/Malaysia/ 2506/2004-like (B/Victoria/2/87\nlineage) and 4 as B/Florida/4/2006- like (B/Yamagata/16/88 lineage). \n\nProfessor Steve Field, chairman of the Royal\nCollege of GPs, said: 'Older age groups are now\nshowing increases, and they have the most serious\nconsequences -- the extra load on the lungs,\npossible viral pneumonia and the increased risk\nof contracting a 2nd bacterial infection.
